RATING: 7
Kemet is a race turning into a tug war later. Player order of prime importance. In the early game you want to be the first to buy the power ups, whereas later you want to be the last to move your armies.

Powering up is great fun, especially if you get the monsters on the board. The tug war part is not so much fun because you are just attacking the weakest army on the board in order to ensure your victory and to secure the victory point you get from winning a battle. 

If only the battle system was a bit more exciting and if weak armies would also have a chance, even a small one, would Kemet be a very good game. Now it is just an average multiplayer tactical skirmish. Long term strategy plays a less important role, too.

RATING: 7
I was very excited when I bought this game. I liked so many things about it: its light war-game nature, the Egyptian mythology theme, the tech-tree that allows you to personalize your faction, the giant monsters following you in battle. Then I played it a few time and the game left me with the strange feeling of a shallow game-play. I felt like every play would be the same, as during the first few turns everyone buys up technologies just to then start teleporting all over the place, targeting the weakest spot to get easy points and conquering temples. It was terribly monotonous.

That made me sad. I hated the teleporting rule and even more the huge impact of the turn order on the entire thing. I never really disliked the game, but I thought it wasn't designed well enough. So I played it a few times, rated it a 5, sold my copy and forgot about it. Almost two years later, I had the opportunity to play it again and I wanted to give it another chance. So it happened that I changed my mind, at least a little bit.

While I still don't love this game as much as many others  here on BGG do, I had a lot of fun. I enjoyed the challenge that the game was able to provide me, the tension and the need to time your actions perfectly to get what you want. I liked to build combos with the technology tiles and personalize my strategy. At the same time though I still think that the teleporting rule is stupid and that the turn order is too relevant. I also still think that every game will start exactly the same with players buying technology tiles as much as possible. And I have another complaint, that only I seem to have with Kemet: while in general the artwork is really well done, I don't like how the board looks like and that definitively lowers my enjoyment of the game and consequently my rating.

Therefore, after so many twists and turns in my opinions, I must say that I enjoy Kemet quite a bit, but I would enjoy it more if the game would have been a little different in some of its aspects. At least though I now understand why people like it so much, even if I don't necessarily agree on everything. Kemet is a pretty good game. I like it!

RATING: 8
FAVOURITE GAME - each player deploys the troops of an Egyptian tribe and uses the mystical powers of the gods of ancient Egypt – along with their powerful armies – to score points in glorious battles or through invasion of rich territories. A game is typically played to 8 or 10 victory points, which may be accrued through winning attacks, controlling temples, controlling fully-developed pyramids, sacrificing to the gods, and wielding particular magical powers. It is a fast paced strategy/wargame, where the emphasis is on direct combat. Building up the defenses will only make you lose. The artwork is great, the rules easy to grasp and it doesn`t take ages to play as similar games do.

RATING: 7
Need a few more plays to rate this one definitively.

Early impressions:
I really want to love this game! However, for me it feels too Euro, although it does a great job to hide that from people: nice artwork, theme connected to game mechanics, a few of the best-looking miniatures I have ever seen in a game... when played, it's Euro all the way. Not that Euro is a bad thing, but it could have opted for a little more 'feel' instead of 'if you add this number and it's higher than his number you win'.
For instance the board looks cool. It's great design creating the spaces as they are: every city at exactly the same distance as everything else... But I don't want that, I want a game to be intuitive, I want to 'see' and 'feel' they are the same distance, not 'accept they are the same distance because the same number of lines run between them'.

Theme (artwork/story/...)
1/2
Gameplay (smooth play/player interaction/strategy&tactics/depth)
5/5
Fun factor (can you afford a smile during play?)
1.5/3

RATING: 7
Feels like a knife fight with four other people in a lift.

It's a pretty simple game that depends on majority on numbers and card play to win. It's a pretty good system that beats dice rolling randomness.

The tiles to boost certain advantage too is a good add on. But I do feel that the 'I bought it you can't have it' idea on the lame side. There will always be certain tiles that are more advantages than the others. It's a matter of circumstances or player interaction, that these circumstances would make other tiles seem just as useful. 

In games where no ones knows each other, the game becomes a killer instinct session with no holds barred. Useful tiles will be the first to go to the lucky chap that gets to decide who goes first. The advantage of going last is only apparent importance near the last 2-3 VP snatch moment.

Good fighting game. No story to tell. Just gather the strongest number and do the necessary. Fight a minor battle to get rid of cards and take back the important ones.

RATING: 8
There's a small feast of options to make Kemet an asynchronous tactical slugfest. Teching up is fun. Combat is bloody and fast. 

Unfortunately, the balance is a tiny bit wonky. I vastly prefer the 10 point game to the 8 point game. But those two points also significantly extend the length of the game. Kemet would be firing on all gears if it nailed the balance between ideal length and strategy.

Kemet is a very good option for a hybrid Euro-Thematic knife fight. The wonky balance of length and choices holds it back the most for me.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7
Kemet is reminiscent of Nexus Ops in that it is a low complexity conflict game whose scoring system actively encourages aggressive play.  The Sanctuary of All Gods is not quite the focal point that is Nexus' monolith, but it's close.

In Kemet three sets of power tiles aligned to different deities are at the heart of the game.  Of course you'll want the ones that bring mythical monster miniatures, but it's fun to experiment with different combinations to give your forces an edge.  The battle cards ask some quite interesting questions about your appetite to inflict losses and preparedness to take some.  Kemet allows you to leader bash, weakling bash and kingmake.  For my taste only the last of those is a significant demerit.  Overall, it's a fun game.

Production quality is good.  The monsters aren't quite on the monstrous  scale of Cyclades, but they're still handsomely done.  The soldier miniatures are less impressive.  The board is ugly, but functional.  The box is a gaudy delight; it doesn't so much stand out on the shelf as leap off it with a wild cry.

RATING: 8
Pros:
- Amazing Quality & Beautiful Miniatures - Feels Bad Ass to command the Legendary Creatures.
- Extremely fast playing (90 minutes or less!)area control game that rewards attacking & discourages turtling (you will lose if you try to turtle).
- Great Card Combat Mechanics make for exciting diceless battles.
- Prayer Resource & Action Management felt appropriate and well developed.

Cons:
- Perhaps too many Power Tile upgrades for your faction, could benefit from fewer choices to speed play and make tiles more equally beneficial.
- Has a bit of a problem with Kingmaking towards end, as players can attack weakened armies after they get out of a battle for cheap easy VPs. Rewards reactionary/opportunistic game play to a fault.
- Some Power Tile Upgrades are far better than others (extra actions, teleports, cost reductions)
- Rulebook requires 2-3 read through, you can miss a few things. Like you cannot have 2 power tiles of the same name, no matter what, not even of different colors. (we played wrong for a bit)

Plays best with 4-5 players. 
Player who makes best tile purchases, wins most battles, and manages their actions best wins!

RATING: 7
We played last night at Rugby on Board. This is pure Eurotrash, (mostly Trash) with a smattering of civ building. It annoyed me in the same way Cyclades did - a semi-random end game which relies upon everyone knowing who is in a position to win, then stopping them. The problem comes that when you attack somebody to prevent the win, you are making it easier for another player to swoop in and take the victory. 

There are also multiple choices available from the start which combined with 5 actions a round, will cripple some AP prone players. 

On the plus side, there is a lot of game there for a relatively short play time. It feels a bit like Nexus Ops crossed with Game of Thrones with a few elements of Through the Ages thrown in. 

I like that it rewards attacking (you get a point for winning a battle - 8 wins the game).

It is a leader in its class - light but meaty battle-focused area control game playable in under two hours [maybe Chaos in the Old World?]

It has less dodgy combos than Cyclades....

Another 'advantage' over Cyclades is that the rather nicely sculpted minis stay on the board rather than disappear into the ether after a turn.

RATING: 9
Kemet is a great looking thematic game filled with many strategical and tactical decisions in every moment within the game. RATING: 9
11-16-2013 Update for Santa:

10 Things We Like About Kemet:
1) Plays well with two -- one of our core criteria
2) Plays well with more -- nice to have! 
3) Pretty. (Minis, board, tiles.)
4) Easy to learn
5) Good depth to time spent ratio. An hour for two feels satisfying.
6) Mechanics feel streamlined and slick. Mechanics flow nicely together, and it's easy to teach and understand. Things feel like they make a lot of sense! Light on luck -- you control your destiny.
7) Enjoyable and logical victory conditions! (You win when you attack more, and attacking more is fun.)
8) Easy to get people to play. Looks exciting. Cool factor. 
9) Each game feels very different. 
10) Direct interaction - gobs of it.  

---

Super fun. Each game is very different, and it is fun to explore new power tile combos. I've played this mostly as 2 player, so as long as we actively explore new strategies, we don't get stuck in a rut. Mechanics are tight and feel streamlined. 

Games always feel close. Plays fairly quick as 2 player, but a pain to set up (good thing we can keep it on the table and would want to play several times before cleaning it up).

Great game.

However, if your play frequency is less than that of others at your table, you will always be at a disadvantage.  So the question comes down to, do you want to memorize all of the tile abilities or not?  I've made my choice.

RATING: 8
Bár nem olyan forradalmi játék, mint amire a vélemények, képek, szabályolvasás után számítottam, de összességében tetszett. Sajnos nehéz elvonatkoztatni attól, hogy az asztalunknál nagyon lesújtó vélemények is születtek (a többségnek nem jött be a játék különböző okokból), és volt aki ennek folyamatosan hangot is adott a játék során, ez egyértelműen kihatással volt azoknak az élményére is, akiknek alapból tetszett. A negatív vélemények címszavakban: "úgy ahogy van nem az én játékom, konfrontatív, és csúnya a tábla"; "túl sok a holtidő"; "királycsinálás". Mi, akik pozitív véleményen voltunk, egyedinek és változatosnak éreztük, sok benne az új ötlet. A konfrontatív jelleg tényleg nagyon erősen megvan benne, de cserébe rövid a játék. A sok holtidő csak akkor van benne, ha valaki sokat agyal a játékon, de normál esetben egy akciókör simán lemegy pár perc alatt. A királycsinálás igaz. A csúnya tábla: nos a nagy tábla tényleg nem szépségdíjas, de az egyéb játékelemek, így a kis táblák is ízlésesek. Összességében inkább mutatós a játék, mint csúnya. Szerintem.
A sok lapka nagyon változatossá teszi a játékot, az akcióválasztás mechanizmusa elegáns, a 7 egyedi lény némi hangulatot is kölcsönöz az egésznek. Az egyiptomi téma szerintem egész tűrhetően átjön. A mozgási mechanizmus, a térkép felépítése és a pontozási rendszer (templomok díjazása) a játékba kódolja a konfliktust. A győzelmi pontok típusait nehéz elsőre átlátni, vannak fix és elveszthető pontok is, pont ez ad a játéknak egy konfliktusos lüktetést, egymás piramisait és a templomokat folyamatosan veszik el egymástól a játékosok, mert az azokért járó pontok nem véglegesek.
Elsőre hibásan játszottunk néhány dolgot. A FAQ elég vaskos, sajnos nem minden derül ki a szabályból (pl. teleport kártya). Szerencsére az ikonrendszer jó, egy parti után szinte minden lapka és kártya jelentése egyértelmű. Összességében ajánlom mindenkinek, aki gyors harci jellegű játékra vágyik. De leszögezem, hogy (szerintem) távolról sem wargame-ről van szó. Kb. annyira harci, mint a Shogun, csak ez színesebb, viszont kevésbé letisztult, kevésbé kiugró. De nem rossz!!!
---
Nagyon nem mindegy, kivel játszik az ember. A második próbára nagy sikere volt a játéknak, ez az én játékélményemet is javította. Nagyon nagy újrajátszási lehetőség van benne szerintünk a sok lapka miatt, rengeteg nyerési utat sejtünk benne. Most már jól látszik a 3 szín jellege: piros lapkák = támadó, kék lapkák = védő, fehér lapkák = erőforrás. Ami furcsa, hogy a páros létszámok esetén nincs a pályán szentély.

Egyik 3 fős rövid parti 40 perc alatt lement!
---
Azért egy hosszú parti el tud nyúlni... 5 parti után eddig mindig más stratégia volt a nyerő. Nagyon sokféle lapkakombót össze lehet állítani, folyamatosan jövünk rá az egyre jobbakra. Néhány lapkával megjártuk már, mert a jó ikonozás ellenére is van néhány kérdés, ami a szabályban nincs benne, csak a játék közben derült ki. Elég széleskörű faq van egyébként a játékhoz. Még egy dolog: nagyon nehéz megbecsülni, hogy kit kell ütni, épp ki áll jól, mivel a vége felé egy körben akár 3-4 pont is szerezhető.
---
Már vagy 10-15 nagyon különböző lapkakombót kipróbáltunk. Az új kedvencem az, amihez nem kell felhúzni 4-esre egy piramist se, így nem kell nagyon őrizni a várost: Charge!+Blades of Neith+Defense!+Legion+God Speed... plusz még néhány egyéb lapka is hozzácsapható, meg persze pár kreatúra, ezzel rengeteg harci GYP szerezhető így, és nem is nagy a költsége. 2 hely tartósan tartható a 6+6 emberrel, mivel minden seregnek +2 ütőereje van.
---
Kieggel is kipróbáltuk, nagyon jó úgy is, még változatosabb. De miért csak 7 pontot adtam én erre a játékra?? Felpontoztam 8-ra, de még a 9 se kizárt!
---
Hihetetlen jó ez a játék, de kipróbáltuk úgy, hogy minden kieget használtunk, és így én már egyszerűen elveszek a sok lehetőségben, nem tudok jó kombót összehozni:) Kipróbáltam a 2 bekezdéssel ezelőtt említett kombót, ami az alapjátékban működött, de itt már annyi plusz dolog bejön, hogy semmire se mentem vele. Sokkal több szörny van már az olcsók közt is, megjelenik az 5-ös támadólap, zsoldosok, plusz a Ta-Seti úton nagyon sok módosítóhoz hozzájuthatunk, megjelennek pl. a papok is, szerintem így nagyon nehéz tuti kombót találni, ami persze a játékot dicséri. Végtelen lehetőség van, nem is tudom, van-e másik játék, ahol ennyire ezt éreztem. És a hatalomlapka készlet mindig teljesen nyilvános, tehát nem attól változatos, hogy mindig más készlettel játszunk, hanem hogy mindig mást veszünk meg, olyan rohadt sok van és annyira sok jó.
A vége nagyon izgalmas tud lenni, bár ezt nem mindig tükrözi a számszerű eredmény. Van olyan, hogy egy körrel a vége előtt nem érezzük, kit kéne ütni, mert látszólag azonos esélye van több embernek is a győzelemre, de lehet hogy a végén az egyikük simán 4-5 ponttal nyer, ami 10-12 pont körüli eredményeknél nagyon sok. Nagyon dinamikusan változik az eredmény, annyi féle győzelmi pont lehet. Sokan kritizálták a játékot a királycsinálás miatt, nos szerintem a fentiek miatt ez ritkán jön elő, pedig mi 3-an játsszuk. Az alapjátékban csomó holtverseny volt, ahol csak a katonai győzelmek döntöttek, a kiegek viszont eddig mindig sima győzelmet hoztak, ha valaki jól fordul rá a végjátékra, lehet hogy már ketten is hiába ütjük. És ezt nagyon nehéz időben felismerni.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-

RATING: 7.5
Great area control game, with an innovative combat system. Choosing the right cards to defend your units, wound other units, or try to win the battle. Combat is so heavily encouraged and there is no turtling possible. On top of that it has giant d4s to represent the pyramids, and awesome miniatures, which represent the monsters you can recruit to fight along side your armies. 
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7.5
Kemet is a gorgeous game: the miniatures are wonderful (different molds have been used for the different armies!), and the illustrations and graphic design are at the same high standard.

Kemet is also an unusual game: it is a crossover of euro and ameritrash, with lots (and I mean lots) of aggression, but also some resource management and power development. There is some luck (in the Divine Intervention cards draw), but the combat system is luckless (with a card system reminiscent of A Game of Thrones).

The rules encourage attacking (and penalize turtling) with a clever mix of temporary (bond to the locations) and permanent victory points, together with easy rules for quick movement.

I am quite fascinated by the design, and I am eager to play it again!
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-

RATING: 6
Game with lots of potential but unsatisfying end game.  To prevent players from simply "swooping in from behind" and gaining a huge benefit from going last on the last turn, try out the following variant:


*** POSSIBLY JUST TRY ONLY ALLOWING ONE TELEPORTATION PER MOVE, AND THAT'S IT. ***


This variant doesn't substantially help the end game IMO.  I want to like this game, a lot, but argh!  Not sure if it will ever hit the table again.

*** If a player starts his turn with 8 VP and no other player has more VP than that player he wins the game. 

This player must have the most victory points.  This check occurs during the player's FIRST action after the Night phase.

Additionally, silver and gold actions occur simultaneously.  So a given troop cannot be moved twice via two tokens during the player's action phase.

Finally, during a given action a troop can only teleport once.***

8 VP is used because this variant will generally add a round or two to the game.

RATING: 7
As an attempt at making a crossover between Euro and American playstyles, Kemet manages to be a very enjoyable game, though leaning just a bit too much towards its Euro side than the other one: miniatures are very nice indeed but, as the game plays and feels, they could be easily replaced by wooden cubes, since the theme’s overall impact on the game is minimal.

Anyhow, Kemet still doesn’t win me over completely as in my opinion it has one big flaw: unlike Cyclades, its replayability is very limited, since the only working strategy is bashing the other players - even better, the leader, just to make sure - while no random occurences happen to make things harder, unpredictable or simply different from game to game, these being dice rolls, events restrictions or reinforcement cards like Cosmic Encounter’s.
You only have to fight the other players over and over, get as many points as you can and buy the best upgrades (always the same ones, mind you) you can get before your opponents do the same.

Sure, I have a good fun whenever I play Kemet, nonetheless I can’t but think it’s still a half-successful attempt at making an interactive eurogame: how it is now, it’s just an euro that scrapped its resource-management while trying to disguise itself as a thematic game.

RATING: 6
Not a bad Americatrash game with European roots, but I had some issues with it, particularly with the end game.

* Because instigating and winning combat gives the player a permanent victory point combat is far more opportunistic than tactical. Find some weak force, such as one that just got out of a fight, and attack it for the vp.

* Some of the buildings seemed to be a a lot better than others. Free actions? Ignore walls?

* The end game had some issues. There is a huge potential for kingmaking, with the final player having a chance to determine the winner by who he decides to attack. The game also rewards a Risk-style final gambit, striking out to get as many points as possible at the last possible minute. The fact that you can hoard the Divine Intervention cards with no limit contributes to this (I'd limit the hand size to encourage their use).

On the plus side, the game did have some great bits and I like the theme of warring Egyptian gods.

RATING: 10
One of the best "dudes on a map" game out there.  The combat system is fantastic.  Do you choose to inflict damage, defend your troops or win the battle?  You can't do all of them ideally.  The game is highly replayable with multiple power tiles to explore.  The map is set up perfectly so that all players temples are equidistant to each other and the other areas you need to control.  The temporary victory points are hotly contested but if you get to wrapped up somebody else might fly under the radar to victory.  This game (more than most others I've played) provides you with a feeling that in order to win you need to out-think and out-maneuver your opponents.

RATING: 9
Wow, I feel like this is, thus far, a vastly underated game.  When I opened the box, I definitely got a Cyclades feel.  There's area control, action allocation, offerings to the gods, MONSTERS, and battles.  However, this game blends it all so well to be completely unlike playing Cyclades (which is also one of my favorite games).  This game is fast and encourages lots of mayhem.  There are numerous ways to customize your faction to your style of play.  Battles are a constant and dependable way to gain victory points, however I won our first game via temple control and permanent victory point power tiles.  The combat is card driven, but it's fast and intuitive and allows for interesting outcomes.  The monsters are very fun and add a lot of flavor to the game without being overpowered.  The downsides are three-fold, for our group.  1) The rule book is a little vague on certain aspects of the game; 2) there is a good bit of iconography that you need to learn on the power tiles and divine intervention cards, and 3) just a little fiddly to set up given all the pieces and tiles.  Fantastic game though, if you like battle games.  We all loved it in a four player session.  I look forward to it hitting the table soon.

RATING: 8
First Play with Isaac and Tyyne. Very cool and complex upgrade mechanic over a fairly simple territory game.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 5
The main neat thing in Kemet is all of the special abilities you can build up for yourself through the course of the game, and this is really where most of the game lies.  You want to assemble abilities that work well together so you can sync up a strategy revolving around those abilities.  Since each player will get different stuff, each player will be approaching the game a little differently.

However, everything else about the game was rather uninteresting.  It mostly revolves around smashing armies into each other to score points for winning battles, and also to secure places on the board that give you points and income (in the form of "prayer points" which allow you to take actions).

The worst part is probably that this is a "race to X number of points" game, where the scores tend to stay pretty close.  So, the final game round degenerates into a kingmaking type deal where everyone analyzes how many points everyone else has just to try to mess with the players in the lead.  Not an attractive quality in a game to me.

I also dislike the combat system used here, where players have a hand of cards and pick what card they will use.  It attempts to be some sort of bluff/double-bluff exercise, which I hate.  Note that Kemet isn't the only game to use this battle system, but it's a mechanic I really dislike.  In this case, there doesn't really even end up being much of a bluff most of the time, as it is often fairly obvious what each side will do anyways.

Overall, just a pretty blah experience for me.  I could take it or leave it if someone else wanted to play, but I'd rather leave it.

RATING: 8
KMC Mini HyperMat, FF Mini Euro 44x68
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8
This, for me, is the best of both worlds. I'm generally more of a Euro engine-building/resource-managing kind of gal, but I really love direct conflict. My problem with most combat games is that often the best way to win is to avoid conflict and focus on defense - Kemet turns that entirely on its head... the only way to win is to play for blood.

And, unlike most combat games the strategy here is more in resource management and timing, rather than strategic movement and placement of units. While this may turn off the vein of gamers that grew up playing Risk, Axis & Allies, etc., I think it's an excellent choice for Euro lovers who are looking to add a little conflict to their lives (me!)

The resource game is excellent. You feel limited in every possible way - in actions, in currency, in units, in map space, in cards. You always want to do so much more than you can, and EVERY decision you make matters. You really feel the weight of every decision's importance in this game. Kemet doesn't fuck around.

Then there's the combat. First, this game uses Victory Points very well. I like the difference between temporary and permanent VP and they way both are used to encourage confrontation. I also really dig card-based combat systems, and this is no exception. Deciding which combat card to discard is always agonizing. There's not a whole lot of room for bluff with your cards but there's [i]just[/i] enough to keep you guessing. Good stuff.

The DI cards add just the right amount of luck to the game. Nothing feels overpowered, and they keep things from feeling too deterministic and keep the game feeling fresh over repeated plays.

So that just leaves the big thing, then: the power tiles. Your first game will be a little overwhelming as it's a hell of a lot to internalize, but these are really the heart of the game. Trying to find new and interesting combinations is fun and denying people their tiles feels great. As with all combo-based games (and ESPECIALLY in a open, perfect-information system like this) I worry about replay value - whether or not after enough plays the game will devolve into just trying to be the first to snatch what you know are the game-winning combos, but it will be just [i]so much damned fun[/i] getting to that point that I have no doubts that I will get my money's worth out of this game regardless.

I find it weird that the minis are of two vastly different qualities. The creatures are beautiful and high quality, but the units are a much cheaper plastic. All my catladies came bent. :(

Edit: This is also one of the few dudes on a map game that is really great 2-player.

RATING: N/A

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 9
Kemet is an extremely interesting and different mix of eurogame and ameritrash which works surprisingly well! The publisher, Matagot, has also released Cyclades which can also be said to be a mix of both genres, though it falls more into the eurocamp than Kemet. There are many similarities: the length and weight/difficulty of the games are similar, the production value is perfect (mainly due to the awesome plastic miniatures in both games). However, in terms of mechanics it’s a very different beast than Cyclades, and I think Kemet is a much better game than Cyclades as the latter feel too static and without too much interaction/combat. I’m a die-hard eurogamer myself, but I think Kemet is the better game. Cyclades is, as said, more in the euro-camp and its main mechanic is centered on auction with a limited amount of combat. Kemet, on the other hand, uses an action point allowance system and is extremely focused on combat between the players. 

The combat works well; it’s simple and elegant and it works much better than the die-rolling combats you see in most games including Cyclades. On the other hand there aren’t big differences as to which cards you play, and it felt a little weird that you can win a combat even though you have lost the most units… But that’s just how it works. And it’s extremely clever that you can attack each other right from the start of the game! You’re able to do so because you can teleport from your home to basically the entire map; maybe it’s not thematic, but I don’t care because the game becomes much better and more alive because of this rule! Now you can attack during round 1, and you don’t have to build up a huge army over several rounds before you think you’re strong enough or even are able to reach your opponents… And you cannot even build a huge army as you have only 12 army units available, which is a good idea. Furthermore, the game rewards attacking and not turtling, which is another good call in terms of the overall design and playability of the game!

The way the game is won is superb. I love that you can play to either 8 or 10 points. The first player to get those points is declared the winner, and that’s a nice change of pace compared to all of those games where you count your points after X number of rounds. As points are public knowledge, the game is constantly moving back and forth as players are constantly attacking the leader each round. The game probably lasts for around 90-120 minutes (maybe longer with 5 players and playing the long game), but it definitely ramps up in speed when you know the various power tiles.

Speaking of these power tiles, they are a big part of why I love the game. The fact that once you buy a power tile, you - and you alone! - have that specific power, is something I’m very fond of. There are 3 kind of tiles; the offensive-minded, the defensive-minded and those which focus more on infrastructe/income. All of them are nice to have, and it’s always a tough choice which to choose! I like that every player start with various levels of these three “tech pyramids” as you’re pursuing different strategies. The multitude of cards and power tiles is great.

Of course there are a few things I’m not thrilled about. I don’t like that other players can take control of your pyramids and limit your options. However, this is a minor concern. My biggest concern is that there might be situations where you cannot avoid kingmaking… This was the case in one of my games as the game was very close, and it was very close to be the case in another game as well. That definitely left a sour taste in my mouth, and if I experience any further incidents like this, I think I’ll have to decrease my rating drastically… But for now it’s a great game which I thoroughly enjoy, and I do suspect that kingmaking issues won’t be too much a problem going forth. One last thing negative, and that's a pretty big one I think; going last is a huge benefit, so if the games are close (like they often are), a player going last has a much better chance of winning, even if he is a couple of points behind before that round! That will be fixed in the upcoming expansion, but that is definitely my biggest problem with the game.

Overall I still love Kemet. Everybody is constantly attacking everybody, but you’re not screwed if you lose a combat or two. The power tiles are awesome. The game plays very differently than most other games I own and love. Kemet is a keeper!

Let's get ready to rumble !!

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 4
While reading through the rules, I was struck by Kemet's similarity to another game which came out that Spiel: Al Rashid. The games do completely different things of course, but still the fact that one takes a simple game core and augments it with a lot of rule modifying and/or enhancing cards [i]is[/i] rather similar. Of course Kemet shares something with Matagot's earlier offspring Cyclades too: quick and tactical combat all over the place. I'm sure that these similarities are coincidental (and perhaps they aren't), but it's still fun to point them out.

Kemet is a VP race where VP are awarded for achieving certain fixed objectives in the game. Winning combat is an important one, but another is for example sacrificing troops on a special shrine. The game is played until a fixed amount of VP has been obtained by one player, and that player will win immediately. Expect therefore a major brawl from about 5 minutes into the game until the end with all the usual bells and whistles: leader bashing, ganging up, killing the weakest player, and so forth. Add to this mayhem the ability of development in three different areas which give benefits in certain ways (offense, defense, income, movement, ...) by collecting these benefits in the shape of usually unique development cards, semi-open battle cards, and a small WP engine to tie it all together, and you've got a pretty good grasp what Kemet is about. The battle cards are interesting: you get a set of six, and upon entering a battle select two, one of which gets discarded face down onto your personal discard pile. The various numbers on the remaining card indicate how many hitpoints and defensive points and whatnotpoints are doled out and received prior to injury; if necessary augmented (or weakened) by developments obtained prior to the combat. The trick is that these six cards are all you get, and if you exhaust your hand, you take up your discard pile. Attentive players will therefore learn over the course of the game what your hand is capable of; it will also shape your own development path.

Kemet is a game revolving solely around combat for combat's sake. This is no place for turtling and building up a position: this is Unreal Tournament in boardgame form. And set in ancient Egypt rather than the future, of course. The rules indicate as much: winners attack. Not mindlessly, of course, but they don't sit still in a corner either. And that is one of things that puts me off. Combat for the sake of combat is not a design pattern I find interesting. Perhaps when we're done in 20 minutes or so, but not when the game takes significantly longer (which Kemet does). The numerous development cards and WP engine are there to increase longevity and diversity, which is obviously a Good Thing. But I can't shake the feeling that it is all about adding up numbers on one of six cards with a few modifiers and comparing these, and then there's a lot of extra stuff in the game for this simple action! This is not meant to be a thinky game, yet it feels at times like it is. This can be good or bad depending on how you usually approach this sort of game. And finally, Kemet's execution is, to my eyes, yucky. The board is too stark and 'hard' in colours and contrasts; and the developments are examples of total form over function. It's what Matagot are famous for, of course, but they went overboard in this design. Cyclades is stylish [i]and[/i] reserved; Kemet is just an eyesore.

In short: while the game offers some great functionality for those who like this combat for the sake of combat-approach, including a semi-deterministic combat resolution and lots of ways in which to specialise, it is not a title made for me. Even though the garish design results in one point deduction, I'm inclined to cancel this in view of the obvious effort to make a not totally looney tactical combat game with a lot of options and possibilities to avoid staleness. But the end note, a 4, does not indicate the same willingess to play as do my other 4's.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-

RATING: 7
The fellow explaining the rules said, "this game will be over in fewer turns than you think." And indeed, it was so.

I'm generally somewhat fond of "let's fight!" games which have something to fight over, and which don't penalize attacking. Kemet was no exception, and I enjoyed most of the mechanics and dynamics. The one exception: it seemed (on one play) like the upside to winning attacks was so high that (a) you nearly always wanted to optimize for "what battle am I most likely to win?" rather than "what battle will best funnel into a larger strategy?", and (b) having the weakest army on the board was asking to be attacked by anyone who could manage it. The latter in particular led to nearly every troop sent out being 5 units strong, ideally with monster backup, and I found the homogeneity of troop size made the game less interesting.

(I'm wondering if our table was overvaluing disbanding troops, overlooking that the action required to get them back on the table could instead be used for 2 prayer.)

Would happily play again.

RATING: 10
Kemet is a gamer's game. There are a lot of moving parts here, so teaching this to new players can be difficult. Once you have a group of experienced Kemet players the game takes off and provides one of the best gaming experiences you will find. It looks phenomenal, moves at a nice pace and is full of "take that" opportunities that aren't too punishing to the other players. It also rewards those who can think laterally and rebound from adversity. It even managed to sneak in a bit of worker placement as the way to decide your actions each phase and I love the fact that turn order can change between each round. More games should implement that idea. Highest possible recommendations, except to new gamers.

RATING: 9.6

RATING: 6
Feels a bit light, random, and over maybe a bit too quickly. Overall, movement is so dynamic that it's not about area control and if you're wanting it to be, you'll be surprised or frustrated. It's about scrapping for fights. But then even the fights themselves are just "play a card and add your unit count" -- and your pool of cards is only six cards. Just not a lot of depth. The tech tree with the power tiles is interesting and does add depth -- I can see how some players could get really into that and path strategies based on it, optimize, etc., but I don't find myself sufficiently captivated by this design to want to spend much time on that. Should give this another shot before I consider trading this otherwise pretty highly rated and high-production-value game. But life is short, and I don't see much more Kemet in my life.

RATING: 8.5
The premier Euro-hybrid dudes-on-a-map game. =-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8
After one game: another very good dudes on a map game. This one encourages attacking very much and therefore there's constant action from the beginning. Combat is based on calculating possible enemy moves and watching opponents' previously played cards so you can predict what they MAY play this time, but it's not 100% sure. And thanks to the divine intervention cards you can never be certain if you win or lose. 

Concerning the basic gameplay Kemet does not outstand that much. You recruit new army(and I like the fact that you can recover nearly your entire army in just one turn - thanks to this the game is very dynamic, units appear on a map quickly... and die even more quickly!), move, attack, pray to gods to get power you can spend... and here comes the thing. Because you can afford not only new units but also improve your empire technologically. Power tiles is where Kemet really shines. There are plenty of them, divided into three colours: red(attack), blue(defense) and white(utility). At the start of a game there's no assymetry, but as it all goes on each player will have different possibilities and specialise in other fields. Some tiles allow you to recruit a creature that has some unique abilities and most of the time help your units to fight more efficiently.

The victory point system is interesting and handled well. There are permanent VPs, gained mainly from winning a battle(but not only), and temporary VPs - for maintaining control of temples on a board, for example. Thanks to that variety Kemet is a deep, complex game as there are always many things to consider. You can even gain a point by buying a proper power tile!

I can't reach verdicts after playing a single game, but I really liked it(despite the fact that we confused many rules and played a bit incorrectly). Looking forward to play it again, unfortunately I don't own the game - but maybe that will change?

RATING: 8
Great, great game, with lots of theme, cool mechanics and even including a taste of wargame with "dudes on a map".
The aim of the game is to develop and gain strenght with our tribe, so that it can be possible to outrun the opponents, either by scoring points with offers to the gods or by winning battles.
There can be few or there can be lots of interactivity, depending if the players engage with each other in battles or not. The battle resolutions depend a bit out of luck (number of troops + power of creatures + power of hidden cards) but the original twist here is that the loosing side may sometimes be the one who profits more from that battle.
Things can be a little chaotic in middle game since that, with the "teletransport" power, attacks can come from everywhere, however, that´s also an element of surprise in the game.
The first few games can be a little hard because there are dozens of tiles with different powers to chose from, however, (and although a bit unbalanced) the powers of the 3D creatures are quite thematic in Egypt mythology and visually apealling.

We're all followers of different gods in the Egyptian pantheon and we're trying to gain the most favor from our gods, represented by victory points in game. If a person has at least 8 victory points at the end of a round, they win. How do you get points? There are a number of ways. You can win battles as an attacker, you can occupy temples, you can research abilities that just give you points, and so on. All while acquiring unique abilities for your army.

RATING: 8
(Alex)
Fast-paced dudes-on-a-map game. It reminds me of mixture of the following games:
- Nexus Ops: achieve certain #VP to win, attacking is rewarded, boost cards and the fast-pace.
- Chaos in the Old World: area control, improve your race according to your strategy, cool minis.
- Game of Thrones: battle resolution playing cards.
- Cyclades: aesthetically, mythological creatures to buy.
Symmetrical game that turns asymmetrical with the development of technologies.
The game is quick, but on first games AP may drag a bit when scanning all technologies available to try to set up a combo/strategy.
Drawbacks (?):
- You can win a battle (and score 1 VP) loosing all your troops whilst enemy troops survive...

RATING: N/A
[2016-01-18 Rating: n.a. First Impression]
Since I don't own this game and I don't know if and when I'll play it again, I wanted to record a short first impression (after a single game). 

While Kemet is at it's heart a dudes on an map (DOAM) game, a genre I despise, my one play of it didn't turn out as bad as I thought it would. The name of the game is attacking since that's one of the ways to generate (permanent) victory points. Going into the game with this in mind, I didn't mind the attacks of the other players too much since it's not like you lose something you've built up for a few rounds. It felt more like your overall strategy is defined by the technologies you acquire and they can't be taken from you. The game play on the map is more tactical than strategic. This became quite clear once we realized that we were playing a key rule wrong: The attacker has the option to take back their units after a victorious battle. Without this rule, no one really wanted to attack because with a weakened army on the map you're just easy prey. With this rule in place, Kemet became more interesting instantly since now you didn't have to fear giving an easy victory point to another player after an attack. With no more easy points to grab on the battlefield anymore, you really had to look hard for good scoring opportunities. Kemet felt a lot more euro like after we implemented this rule.

Nevertheless, I don't think that Kemet will ever become a favorite of mine. There's still the opportunity for king making which I could observe first hand in my game and I still don't like the genre. But if someone in my group would want to play a DOAM game, I would propose Kemet.

RATING: 8
Kemet is invariably compared to Cyclades although they are actually very different games. Kemet uses a huge tech tree to generate combos and big moves, Cyclades uses auctions and one off cards that rotate ala Stone Age. 

They're both area control but Kemet is more fluid. The game encourages attacking by awarding VP's for it and by the use of a brilliant map that puts everyone at the same distance from everyone else.

Despite the differences, if you like one, you'll almost certainly like the other. Personally I prefer Cyclades but I'm always happy to play either.

RATING: 8.75
Really good and fun game. Lots of decisions to make both strategic, in terms of how you will develop your tile powers and to what purpose, and then lots of tactical opportunistic decisions as to how to grab (and maybe hold) those VP's.

Combat is dice-less but is not deterministic and is a tense affair with nice twists like winning the battle but taking too many hits. The game is dynamic and encourages the attack over defence but without ever making defence a complete waste of time. The monsters are just plain fun and the board which makes everyone's position equal in distance and strategically is a work of genius.

All of this with 8 pages of rules - and great artwork and components. Very good.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 6.5
Wanted to like this one a little more than I did.  It's a fine fantasy battle type thing.  I guess a lot of the stuff just felt kind of limiting: the money; the stacking & piece limits on your forces; the bank of possible upgrades, most of which are unique, and all first-come-first-serve; but most of all, the deterministic combat.  I've written about this before, but Kemet at least does it a little better than most.  You have several options on your cards, whether you're going to try and win the battle, damage your opponent's troops, or protect yours from harm, and the usual tradeoffs between them.  Trouble is, it's not usually that interesting.  If you have roughly even forces, you'll each want to play your highest card.  If your opponent outnumbers you enough, you can't win, even with your highest card.  So, you'd want to play a damage card to at least hurt him, but then he'll play a block card, and so forth.  And that's before you factor in the upgrades, many of which give you permanent advantages in battle.  If you fall behind in those, you may discover that you can't win no matter what you do, as your foe has +3 to every combat.  There are a few of the special cards that add to battle totals, so at least that capability exists.
I appreciate a lot of the design.  It's very tight, it doesn't run too long, all the locations are accessible, and so on.  That last, in particular, contributes a lot to the flow of the game and prevents turtling.  Teleport spots litter the field, so as long as you've got a little magic to spend, you can go practically wherever you want.  As cutthroat as it is, you still can't just waltz into someone's base, since the walls stop you for a turn (which the other player can use to prepare).  You can, of course, invest in siege tech to get around this.
There are other oddities.  The fluidity of movement, plus the piece limits and combat from earlier mean that if you lose a battle, you usually want to sacrifice your last couple guys rather than retreating them.  If you leave them out on the board, another player's liable to swoop in and pick them off, and the teleports are one-way, so you can't recall them home.  Reinforcing might be an option, but it's usually just as easy to disperse them and re-summon back at base.
I don't dislike it, but at the same time feel like there might not be enough there to hold my interest once I've explored a couple of the upgrade paths.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 6
After 1 play:

I wanted so bad to love this game, but I felt let down after my first play. 
This game had everything I could ask for in a game: combat, card play, tech, and a wide variety of strategies to play with. But instead it came across as a game that had its does in every pool, but not enough to satisfy.

ITS A WARGAME!
Yeah, kind of. Because everyone has the same cards the battles seem to lack the unknown that games like Cosmic Encounter or Dune have (my two favorite games). I found that battling with less than 5 troops was pointless, there was just no chance of an underdog win.

You also miss out on feinting (sp?) attacks because of positioning since everyone can always attack everyone.

ITS A WORKER PLACEMENT GAME!
No, it's not. A good worker placement game has blocking of opponents. Here you only have to make about one choice in actions in the top row. It works, but it doesn't seem to be a great fit. I think I like how Chaos in the Old World handles a similar system better. Where you just do things until you run out of magic...or whatever.

SO WHATS GOOD?
I like the techs that give you plenty of routes to upgrade. The minis and art are good (not mind blowing). It's relatively quick. But the best thing this game has going for it is timing. If you're not trying to figure out your opponents next few moves or techs they're going after, your going to struggle. Getting the timing of events down is key to this games success and enjoyment.

In the end it was average for me. I'll play it again, but I won't push for it. I rarely felt clever during the game and the battles never felt as exciting as I thought they would. I think this game is a little over hyped.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-

RATING: 6
The main thing Kemet has going for it is speed and ease of play. Some players can dither over all the upgrade tiles but generally there's no reason why it should take you more than a couple of minutes per player turn. RATING: 8.5
Awesome game.

Think of this as a tactical light wargame set in Heavy Metal Ancient Egypt, with the best mechanisms from Euro games and the ability to make your faction completely unique and asymmetrical.

There is the mother of all upgrade shops on the side of the map. Most upgrades are unique. A lot of these upgrades give you mythical monsters (complete with minis). 

The map is incredible - because it somehow manages to make every space no further than 2 moves away from every other space - and yet it looks like a map of Egypt.

Oh, and YOU MUST ATTACK.

Fail to attack and you've lost a precious opportunity to earn points. First to 8 points wins.

Awesome game.

Most of my mates would rather play Kemet than Chaos in the Old World. 

Only criticism - 2 of the upgrades seem overpowered, so we've just removed them from the game.
-White Tier 1 Upgrade (All purchased upgrades are $1 cheaper)
-Red Tier 4 upgrade (Kill 2 dudes before combat starts)

RATING: 6
Kemet is a euro-ameritrash hybrid. It has a lot of plastic components, direct confrontation, and creates incentives to initiate conflict. On the other hand, it has a tech-tree, action points, the winner is determined by VP, and the card combat is fairly deterministic. I can see why this game appeals to many people. It doesn't really appeal to me on a component level since I actively dislike unpainted plastic minis. Beyond that, the tech-tree can be make players be prone to some analysis paralysis, especially due to the iconography and lack of player aids. I recognize this is a solid design and I certainly like it better than Cyclades, but I think the perfect euro-amertrash hybrid is out there somewhere and I'm going to hold out for it rather than keep Kemet.

RATING: 9
Glen & Richard own this. This is one of the best area control, combat-oriented games that I have played. It incentivises attacking and has a catch up mechanic for the player with lowest VPs (they choose turn order and going last is a big advantage), Building up the tech trees and all paths are possible. I've had two four-player games and it seems that dominating at least one of the tech trees (white - economy, blue - defence, red - attack) and utilising its strengths while not making yourself the target for the other players are the keys to victory.

RATING: 7
It takes some getting used to because [i]everything[/i] is done differently than in other games and you need the manual for all the symbols. But after a few plays it should move quicker. I really like the little rules tweaks to make it an offensive game. Superb production!

The main reason I don't rate this higher is because of the rather steep hurdle to get into the game. You have access to all the power tiles right from the beginning and since there are many you can only get once and all their functions are explained through icons, you basically have to explain every one to newcomers or they might feel cheated.

RATING: 7
Very pretty, low complexity area control wargame. an excellent effort that has a broad appeal. The winner is the person who sticks to a consistent strategy and can husband their score well enough not to wear a neon sign declaring they are too far ahead.

Turn order and resource management are key. Tracking of victory point sources is very well done in a manner that initially seemed superfluous, but proved to be crucial information when vying for the win.

Attacking is not only encouraged but rewarded as a primary source of VP. Area control is also rewarded at key locations with synergistic bonus VP.

Very cool, best of all it is not related to Cyclades as I feared.

RATING: 7.5
Winning is impossible without conflict.  And I like the way it is handled.  No dice rolling, uncomplicated & quick.  Creating a strategy is important.

 The tiles in game to game stay the same (much to my pleasure) so that with a few plays we know what is available and which combinations might be good to try.  It is easily learned although I kept forgetting all the minor ways victory points were won, so I had to be told I won when I didn't even realize how, and had to ask.  Temporary points do not have to be converted to permanent ones in order to win!  Permanent/temporary VP's is another interesting aspect, unique in games, for me.  

Art work & components are great, the board is easy to read as are the cards, tiles, & player's boards.

RATING: 8.5
Pros
-Moderate game length
-Supports different number of players via different boards
-All players equal distance to each other player
-Card based combat rather than dice, can have impact even with small army/losing
-Game rewards offense (which also nearly sets a definite end game)
-Unique and interesting tech tracks
-Can acquire monsters, miniatures for them are pretty good
-Lots of viable strategies
-Turn order done a bit awkwardly

Cons
-Possible king making
-Small army sizes, winner of combat is typically whoever has 5 units + monster
-Players do not have inherent unique powers (minor)
-Could use a larger pool of combat cards? 6 feels repetitive but makes it more predictable

RATING: 7
The three technology trees provide an entertaining method of alternate victory strategies, even if the game centers around fighting. It strikes me that a particular power which makes each power cost one prayer point less is ridiculously good and difficult to pass upon as player 1 and player 2, but that may be inexperience talking.
The battles really work out because they center around two different goals: Winning the actual fight and keeping units alive to hold their location. The divine intervention cards provide a satisfying, but not unmanageable, amount of randomness to the game, since if a player really stockpiles them the player becomes weak once all those cards have been used.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8
A fantastic Ameritrash wargame! There are just so many components to this game that I love: the upgrade system, the battle system, the VP system, etc. Play felt very well balanced. Decisions were many and it was always a struggle to figure out which move was the best at any given time given how many things could be done that would help one's goal overall. BEAUTIFUL looking game! Simple to understand and yet it feels like a very in-depth game. The game is set up so that there is constant fighting going on - it is not a game where anyone can turtle away and build up VPs in any way that they will win. This game promotes action. Iconography works. I can't say enough good things about this game. It is one of those games where you will say, "if only I had one more action!" Amazingly, none of the mechanics are new, but Kemet still comes off as innovative and distinct.  I am after several plays feeling a bit raw over its "eat and be eaten" style of gameplay.  It definitely is a game that allows at points for some bash-the-leader and king-making situations, and I'm not a fan of that style of game personally. It's the one kink in my overall enjoyment of Kemet. But it's not a fault of the game; simply a part of the gameplay that doesn't totally gel with me personally. But all in all, it's one of my favourite dudes-on-a-map games.

RATING: 2
The production of the game for me was an immediate warning.  This game was a little to garish than I normally like - it blinded like an airbrushed van with wizards and scarabs and scorpions in a sand storm, Oh my!!  

The game play didn't impress either as it felt a mess of  mechanics that ultimately funnels toward an uninteresting combat system (which is the core of the game).  I add my points you add yours - resolve.  Sounds simple enough, but with so many bonus tiles, cards etc. after finally tallying and comparing you often find yourself either stalemated or picked apart and neither beaten or victorious (leaving you ripe for easy conquest from the next player in turn order).  

The latter issue just leads to people hesitating to fight - creating the oft-maligned action of combat avoidance and slowly grabbing VPs from safer outlets.  

A boring and mechanically infantile game I wish to never play again.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7
Kemet is a game in which player battle each other until one player have 8 (or 10) victory point.  Some VP are permanent and some are temporary.  The permanent VP cannot be lost whereas the temporary can (most of the time its because your opponent take it from you).
Each player have an individual player board on which they select their action.  Actions includes :
- Recruit unit
- Move unit (attack if an opponent is on the same spot)
- Pray (to gain prayer point which are needed for some of the actions)
- Raise a pyramid (each player have a pyramid of each color that allow them to buy powers of that color)
- Buy a blue, red or white power (or creature)

You can gain Permanent VP by :
- Winning battle while being the attacker (if the defender wins, he don't get VP)
- Gaining a VP power tile
- Controlling the Sphinx
- Controlling 2 temples at the end of a turn
- Sacrificing 2 units in the Sanctuary of all Gods

You can gain temporary VP by :
- Raising a pyramid to level 4 (or controlling an opponent's one)
- Controlling temples

I like all the different powers.  They all seem great but you can't have them all.  The creatures also have special power that strengthens your troops!

RATING: 8.2

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7
Interesting game of warfare with Euro mechanics and fantastic miniatures. Combat is at its core, and almost everything you do outside of attacking your opponents works toward improving your odds in battle, directly or indirectly. That happens by the acquisition of special power tiles, including mythical creatures, and those tiles are the soul of the game. But although the rules are simple, the sheer number of special powers may be intimidating to new players.

The game works, but with the easiness to teleport troops to points of interest and the ability to "recall" them after battles, it is a bit strange, with most areas of the map being always empty. Defending your position is extremely hard and there is barely any tactical allocation of troops. Also, a lot depends on turn order, which is oddly chosen every round by the player in last place. It often feels a little scripted, as players tend to leave their attack actions to the end of the round, in order to take advantage of newly acquired special powers and to reduce the risk of being attacked. Those flaws prevent me from rating it higher, but it is still a solid design.

RATING: 7
A light area-control game emphasising battles between players. Rather cheap aswell. Unfortunately the fight system is simply too light. It doesn't seem to matter at all which cards you decide to play since the opponent can choose between beating you in strength this turn or taking out your units to beat you in the next. There is hardly any strategy or tactic here.

Everything else I love about the game. Teleportation ensures that no one is safe and the fact that victory points can be stolen pushes players into open conflict. But the battle card system feels very incomplete. That's a big con for a game that is all about battles. After just 2 plays I felt like I had seen everything the game had to offer, all power tile combos included. The power tiles are very simple too, by the way. I played with two AP-prone casual gamers and both of them got the hang of them in a few turns.

RATING: 8
This game is Cyclades' goofy little brother. Even the theme is a lot more wacky. They have a similar feel, with ancient battles and mythical creatures coming to your aid. The giant d4s being used as pyramids is an inspired choice. 

But while I love Cyclades, I only like this game. Though they do have a lot of mechanical similarities, but the main difference is the removal of the auction system and the addition of drafting instead. I do love those tiles that are drafted. Every single one seems extremely overpowered, which makes for wonderfully difficult choices. 

However, kingmaking and runaway leaders seem to be a problem in this game, whereas Cyclades always feels a lot tighter to the end of the game.

RATING: 8.5
First of all this is one of the most beautiful game of all time. Just look at this outstanding box cover! I watched a gameplay video and decided to buy it. The rules are quite simple but the game offers some depth. It is a huge advantage that finally i have a game where something is actually happening on the board. I mean the interaction is not just that im taking the tile before you. After unboxing the game still surprises me with it's detailed board and the miniatures however unfortunatelly the game quality is far from good. It come by a broken red miniature, one of the black army's unit is from the other faction but colored as black, the board has a small dent. If you are going to buy a product of matagot be aware of such things. The game itself is outstanding and i believe it will make us really good moments when we are playing with it. The game seems to be balanced out quite well. You are constantly thinking which power tile and which action should i buy. Every single turn there is a movement and a ton of fight otherwise you won't get the nececary VP points. There are a lot of options and way to achieve victory but if you loose it is still fun to do. The theme is one of the best they can choose from i appreciate they choosed it.

Pros:
-One of the most stellar graphics of all time.
-Easy rules, fun gameplay, but the options have depth in them.
-Finally a game where something is happening. Fighting all over the place.
-A great theme which manages you will fall into love with it in your first game.

-The quality of the product is under everything i have ever encountered. Be aware of broken miniatures, dented board.
-For that price i believe there are some better price / value games out there.

RATING: 9.78
If ever there was a game made to teach you that “he who hesitates is lost”, it’s Kemet. In a game where heathen Egyptian deities teleport from temple to temple scourging the victory points from them, where the only thing better than having a giant scorpion is also having the fire pigeon, placing one action marker badly is like stepping off a shinkansen to watch a leaf drop: strangely moving if anyone else was in the vicinity.

Because Kemet is all about the snowball effect. Some games are about, and I am forced to appear gauche here, ‘earning a well-balanced number of victory points toward your vaguely liberal arts degree’. Not Kemet. Kemet is about showing up to the party first, with the best hardware, then shooting everyone else as they ring the doorbell. If you aren’t catapulting yourself exponentially higher with each action, you’re losing. A play of Kemet is short and quick, like nothing so much as a hand grenade in a bathroom. I shit you not, this is cage fighting done right.[color=#ffffff]a9Z4MnAx[/color]
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 2
Last Played: 2014
Plays: 3
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 10
To me, this is pretty close to a perfect confrontational board game, and that's coming from someone who does not really love confrontation much. There are some flaws though:

They could have included more reference guides in the box (we played most of a game with all of us passing one reference booklet around until I realized I should make photocopies). It's minor, but it does slow the game down quite a bit since people can't choose their tiles while waiting for their turn. Make photocopies, problem solved. 

Combat is a little odd. You're dealt 6 random cards from the beginning of the game and you're stuck with them (unless we played incorrectly). So there's a whole other pile of battle cards that's never touched. So if your six cards aren't great, tough luck. It seems like it'd be easy to house-rules that (which we might do in the future). It's a pretty minor problem, I just think it's an odd rule choice. 

Otherwise, it's a wonderful game. I thought it'd be on the lighter side, but I actually found it to be somewhat heavy. Your options are WIDE OPEN at the beginning of the game, which can be slightly overwhelming. However, even our non-gamer friends had a great time, so I don't think things are so overwhelming that the game becomes unfun. I think this has a lot to do with the fact that there seemed to be very few bad choices to make, even though there were a lot of them.

The end of the game was tense. All players were within 2 points of winning, and it just came down to winning a battle (1VP) in a temple (1VP). It was exciting. We all had radically different strategies (I was red, one player played blue, and one player played white until his epic 1 turn blow out), and yet they all seemed fairly viable. 

The temporary victory point thing was great, and really captured the "changing tide" of battle. The miniatures are amazing. I've painted my Mice and Mystics minis, but these don't need to be painted. The artwork on the board and player cards is excellent. Overall, great game.

RATING: 7
A game that works unusually well for a multi-player wargame, mainly because the damage that you can do to an opponent is limited to a handful of hit points. It also combines an engine game element, where you build your economy (in this case by building up prayer points) by buying special tiles from a supply. Since VPs are gained by attacking and are mostly permanent, an early lead is probably going to be decisive unless trailing players conspire to gift one another easy victories. I preferred this to the similar Eclipse as you can't be arbitrarily knocked out of the game at the whim of an opponent. Recommended for lightweight finish-in-an-evening wargaming.

RATING: 7
A pretty solid dudes-on-a-map game. This reminds me first and foremost of Nexus Ops, in that it seeks to overcome the problem of turtling by rewarding you for winning any battle. This works in the sense that no one turtles, but as with Nexus Ops, IMO it creates a thematic disconnect whereby attacking piddly little forces is rewarded as much as or more than holding important territory. 

I also get the sense the that 30 or whatever special power cards in Kemet are pure window dressing. That is, it seems like players put a lot of thought/effort into (1) managing resources (prayer points) so that they can get certain special powers, (2) deciding which special powers would be most advantageous, and (3) working to remember how all the special powers affect battles and resource management. Whereas I think if you stripped away all the special powers you'd see that the design is mostly about take-that and take down the leader -- neither of which I dislike per se, I just feel like why bother obscuring it with all these powers. But perhaps this is a general issue with dudes-on-a-map games. 

In any case, Kemet plays pretty quick, the rules are pretty intuitive, and the minis are great. Next time I'm getting that giant scarab beetle.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 6
I think I just don't like DoaM. The researches add an interesting twist, but adding an interesting twist to a game whose core I don't like won't change much unfortunately.

Update: got a chance to try this again. I'm still fairly convinced that the genre just isn't for me and therefore I have a hard time saying how good an example this is of a DoaM game. Personally, the thing that bothers me the most is how much the game hinges on players policing the game themselves, both in choosing attacks and in setting turn order and so if certain people aren't always thinking about who's winning then some dumb stuff can happen. But if they are doing their diligence than it can feel like bash the leader. So I'm not sure there is a satisfying answer to that for me.

Also, the game heavily incentivizes attacking and also makes you value your dudes very little, which is thematically appropriate but makes the game really weird. It was very rare for people to leave any of their guys on the board after a battle rather than just take them back and get the resources to re-summon them at a temple where they could then teleport. This also lead to this odd situation where there was a standoff of everyone delaying their move actions to the last actions of the round so as to not give anyone any early openings for attack. This just made turn order adjudication that much more important.

The game has it's charms and I can see myself sort of enjoying this occasionally, but having no interest a lot of the time.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-

RATING: 5.5
2015-01-27

Initial Rating: 6.5 (January 2015)

As many friends speak highly of the game, I was expecting something a bit better, still it is a fun game I would play from time to time. The game components are very good as is usual from Matagot, love the miniatures. The play is usually fast if all the players know the tiles, otherwise the downtime can be high. Easy rules.

I enjoy that the game encourages players to attack early making the game very interesting, however nowadays I (generally) don’t enjoy games in which all other players have to attack the leader or lose the game, but this one is of the best I have played in that category. The obvious strategy is to have a low profile for most of the game, letting other players lead while you prepare for your big move near the end of the game. Having saying that, unlike more games, it isn’t necessary a bad thing when you get attacked multiple times (thus the high rating from me). Lots of opportunities for King Making on Kemet, a negative for me.
 
Bottom line, Kemet is  an ok to good game that has a chance to get an improved rating with more plays, however I don’t have any compulsion to buy it because “attacking the leader” is one of my less liked mechanisms. Much better than Cyclades though. 

Current Rating: 6.5

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 9.5
Amazing area control game. Unlike others you're encourage to battle constantly and rewarded for it more than if you try to stay out of trouble in build your economy. You still have to make strategic choices and plan things out in advance. But that plan inevitable involves plenty of war. 

The board is brilliantly designed so no-one has a worse starting position. The tech tree is sprawling and appears well balanced. Every time we think there's a broken strategy someone finds a counter. The different miniatures for each race and the monster figurines are great. It's a beautiful sight on the table.

I only didn't give it a 10 because the turn order does have a big effect on the round. First grants your access to first pick on the tech tree. This is a minor advantage (as every choice has a counter). However, going last allows you to take pretty much whatever area you want uncontested (assuming you planned a head) as your last move. 

This wouldn't matter that much, but the person in last place decides the turn order. They often put the person doing best in last as that's usually the worst position to be in a board game. I've only played it a handful of times and I imagine this problem with disappear with more plays, so I may well increase it to 10 later.

RATING: 7
I'm not sure what to call this game. When I think "war game," I think big sprawling maps and chits and counters and rules. This isn't that. It doesn't seem like an area control game either, because you don't win by controlling territory. This game is a high conflict, area conquering (but not holding) slugfest. It was great with 4 players, not so much with 2. I suspect it would be fine with 3 and good/great with 5, but we'll see. It's tons of fun.

Update: I've dropped my rating to a 7. I really loved my first play of this game, but I keep discovering how group dependent it is. I still think it's amazing, but I want to play it less and less the more I realize that I only rarely have the right group.

RATING: 9
Played it for the firs time tonight. 5 player game. A little long due to being a first play, but 4 of 5 people loved it and want to play some more.

A nice combination between Euro (worker placement, development), and Ameritrash (direct conflict, combat), Kemet is a light skirmish game, with elements of force development, area control, and resource management. 

It plays fast, and encourages player interaction in a clever system of having both permanent and temporary victory points.

Beautiful components, and lots of them, and a ruleset that is learnable in a single play, tremendous amounts of fun, and, I am sure, a playtime that will drop into the 90 minutes or so the box claims all combine for a game I am sure will become a staple in my rotation.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 9.5
Played once and loved the variety of powers and different ways to score points. Thanks to the volatility of the temporary victory points, games can come down to the wire and lend themselves to surprise come-from-behind moments. Loved every part of it and can't wait to play it again. 

---

Four plays in and the rating has increased for me. 

Kemet plays at a great speed, and keeping pace is one of the keys to victory. If you don't keep your foot on the gas, you're going to get left behind in the race for points. Given the variety of ways to get victory points (both temporary and permanent), you've also got to keep your options open and have multiple irons in the fire - so that you can capitalize on any opportunity. The leader can change at a moment's notice, and it's important to keep everyone else's status in mind to keep your opponents in check. And to allow the person in last place to determine turn order for the next round... I could go on and on.

Love this game and can't imagine ever turning down a chance to play.

RATING: 6
I like Kemet. Not enough to rate it an 8. However, it does something that means I do think it deserves such a high score: it incentivizes aggression. Especially how everything, thanks to a teleporting mechanic, is only (at most) two spaces away.
On that front it's very unique in my collection. Makes games go fast and rewards good plays while also creating opportunities for players to do well. 
Also, do love me some nice mythological themes.

Not as good as Cyclades though. Cyclades ends with a bang. Kemet ends in a whimper ("Well, I can't stop either of you! Bah, humbug...")

RATING: 8.3
I like this game but if you are playing a 5 player game and there is a new player it really bogs the game down. When they see all the upgrades they get intimidated and panicked. If everyone knows the game it goes on much better. I like the fact that the upgrades in this game much more and they are accessible if you have a pyramid of that color and level. You can choose to ignore low level upgrades and focus on getting the expensive ones or you might get white tiles that make things cheaper and play the economy game. The thing I don't like about the game is that when you pray it feels like you did nothing and you wait once more for other players to play their turns. It creates a lot of downtime for you while everyone does much cooler things. I also have issues with the card combat system where things become if I am losing I will try to kill all your guys thing. For example in Blood Rage when you lose a battle it doesn't feel like personal while in this they purposely destroyed your units. Overall I prefer Blood Rage over this in terms of accessibility, game length and enjoyability so I give this a 8.3 .

RATING: 10
Love this area control/war game; It has great battle mechanics that doesn't use the RNG of dice but rather a clever card system that works out great since you have to give up one card and play another until your deck runs out. With a bit of sleight of hand that may trick up your opponent. For example you have 2 vs 4. Your opponent may think they have it in the bag but low and behold you just played a card that's higher attack and you also defend damage and better yet you have these card called divine intervention and now you don't take any damage as well or you make your opponent automatically lose some of his dudes so now he even less to fight with. plus a great system that allows the loser of the fight to move a space or run away like a coward all the way back to base but hey, no shame in being a coward because the game allow you to get prayer point which you need to get things done in this game just for doing that!
Depending on what strategy your going with you start the game with your choice red/blue/white/black(if you have the expansion)powers that allow you to make you have better abilities that get even better as you upgrade your powers.Things like getting more troops for the price of less or just gain a victory point because why not? Other joyful abilities allow you to have creatures of egyptian mythology at your troops side to boot which is nice because they stick around even if you've loss the fight they just go back on their respective card until you have more dudes to bring out. 
So much love about this game, it's phenomenal, and I highly recommended it for anyone's board game collection.
