RATING: 7
Here's the thing: I love the mechanics of this game so much that they defeat the memorization and analysis paralysis aspects. 

Kemet's mechanics are smart and they yield a different play experience every time. Yes, certain moves are highly preferred (especially Level 3 conditions from each color), but aside from White's free pyramid advancement card nothing gives a player a runaway advantage. Furthermore, this game is the perfect anti-turtle-er - you never have reason to sit in one place once you max your troops there. Plus, there's a variable base of Victory Points in play at any given time, meaning even someone's sure victory can be quickly disassembled in a handful of moves.

Playing out of those mechanics at the table that drags this down at bit. You have to have all three colors of tech tree tiles committed to memory to make good choices from the beginning (or, constantly have your nose in the reference sheet). Furthermore, even after you have all the possibilities memorized or have a good crib sheet, you must constantly survey your opponents' tiles to understand their shifting powers - this is a major pain. I'm considering making a tablet of Clue-esque tally cards to keep track of an opponents permanent battle bonuses granted from their tiles.

On the plus side, there is no luck present here aside from the initial turn order and distribution of Divine Favors, which is pretty remarkable! I'm strongly in favor of the idea of a fixed bank of battle cards that endlessly recycles with further stratification in both the cards and creatures. (The Cyclades crossover expansion helps with this slightly by doubling the available creatures.)

On the downside, high quality components are plagued by a few bad design decisions. The Divine Favor cards have the same background on the front and back, making it a chore to sort through a stack where some are reversed. Action tokens bounce and roll across the room after the slightest nudge - no game material of that size and heft should be perfectly round! The game board feels overly large for how many units will need to occupy each territory, and the art is overly sharp and contrast-y compared to smooth illustrations elsewhere.

Most-crucially, the battle cards should have been cardboard, linen, or even a non-fraying plastic or Tyvek. These are a mere six cards per player that will likely get handled extensively, slammed down, and picked back up in every round of every game. Leaving aside the lack of randomness from seeing the same worn cards over and over, I simply don't think these 30 cards are built to withstand the level of play they'll be seeing as people become addicted to this game! While I'm happy to throw protectors on them, given the game mechanics they should have been something other than uncoated playing cards.

Kemet is a nice counter-balance to Cyclades - way more options and rules, but constant action instead of biding your time and bidding on gods. However, get ready to settle down for lengthy sessions in your first handful of plays as people spend minutes at a time pouring over the tile options in each round.

RATING: 7
Seems like a refined Nexus Ops with Cyclades' production, but with a bunch of up front complexity thrown at you in the form of stacks of power tiles. It seems like a better game than Nexus Ops to me but the complexity of absorbing all the power tiles basically makes it a multi-game commitment with the same people to get the most out of it. I'd recommend it if you have a group that isn't so large your opponents are different every game, and you like to beat up on each other. I'd have to teach this game probably 10 times before I got a game where everyone was up to speed, and I don't see that being worth it.

RATING: N/A
In Kemet, players each deploy the troops of an Egyptian tribe and use the mystical powers of the gods of ancient Egypt – along with their powerful armies – to score points in glorious battles or through invasion of rich territories. A game is typically played to 8 or 10 victory points, which may be accrued through winning attacks, controlling temples, controlling fully-developed pyramids, sacrificing to the gods, and wielding particular magical powers.

You can definitely feel the shared DNA that Kemet shares with it's cousin [gameid=54998]. I must say though that for all of the improvements to the game I really wish they would have balanced out another path to victory with the Attacking/Red strategy. I will say that overall it is more streamlined game than [gameid=54998], but in this case that doesn't necessarily make it better. If given the choice between the two I am pretty sure I would Kemet's older cousin. Components and artwork are all great, and the cardplay is interesting. I just wish they would have made the game a little bit more rounded that what it is.

Pros:
+ Fast turns. 
+ Very desirable powers on tiles.
+ The rules are quite streamlined. 
+ Nice decisions during combats (choice of cards).
+ The minis are nice looking (painted a bit) and are really used (instead of being only used sparingly as in Cyclades, or of sitting on the side of the board too much like in Blood Rage).

Negative points:
- Since combat requires decisions, they are a bit long for the players who are not involved. Nothing too bad, though. 

We did not fight for pyramids. Maybe we missed something, maybe this is not so relevant?

I find it richer than Cyclades, which revolves a bit too much on auctions instead of actions, and where creatures don't stay as long on the board. Since Cyclades does not work so well with two, maybe I should have this instead of it.

RATING: 9
My favorite "dudes on a map" game. It's just modern and smart all the way down and has so many satisfying and fun things to do, great double and triple bluffs in the combat, and ends while everyone is still having fun (unless you play with 5). The map feels so open and like a big sandbox to mess with your friends on, and you can explore a different "build" of power tiles every game.

Three tips:

1. Don't let the player in the lead go last.
2. Don't just build up power tiles, or it will feel like a sedate Euro.
3. Print a power tile reference for every player, because for some stupid reason the game only includes one copy.

RATING: N/A
Really a gamer's game of wargaming - it trims out all the boring parts, there's no multiple turns of slowly moving into position, and that not wanting to be the first to start the shit-slinging, it skips that.  The game doesn't drone on in the end, all the fun already happened but the game's not finished yet, it ends as you're still having fun, before you're finished with it, and you want to start again.  But it's difference from Cyclades and makes it a "gamer's game" (versus something more accessible to get someone new to a wargame to try) is that it has so many different options for strategies, and is so tightly efficient, that if you spend the first few turns just flubbing about and not having a set strategy yet, you've wasted half your game to no avail.

Seems great, very interested, but would definitely only be for those that love systems and figuring out how to work them into winning strategies.

RATING: 9
It’s a nice feeling when reviews on a new game give you that excited feeling that somebody made a game especially for you, and they made it well. No turtling, short play time, strategy plus lots of clobbering, and a great presentation to wrap it all up. It’s even better when the game does not disappoint after you made the leap and bought it, especially when a target of less than 10 new purchases per year is limiting your margin for error.

An immediate hit in our group, Kemet is a mixture of innovation and thorough development. Things flow smoothly once you've grokked the symbols for the action cards, and I could not think of anything that does not work as well or better than I expected from reading the rules. Admittedly, the 4-cost power tiles don’t see such a lot of action, but I guess that the longer game (10 instead of 8 VP) would rectify that. Since it’s not a real problem right now – still too much to explore at the lower tiers of power – we’ll see about that in good time.

For now, I can just heartily recommend Kemet, especially to anyone who is intrigued after reading the rules or a review, but still on the fence. I held out for over a year in the hopes that I would have an occasion for a test run, and really should have caved in a good deal earlier.

RATING: 5
Pros:
- Visuals great: map, men, creature models, art style on cards.
- I liked the cycle of sacrificing and resurrecting men.
- I liked the pyramid structure for move choices and constraints.

Cons:
- Last couple rounds in the game I found choices really narrowed: all cards were bought, all pyramids upgraded, prayer points no longer being spent as quickly as they're acquired... so 5 turns really only gave 2-3 useful moves (moving twice, possibly resurrecting). Having both silver and gold tokens for extra moves didn't improve things either. Because of this, ending felt repetitive rather than exciting.
- Ending of game also dragged because of back-and-forth for temporary points, as people tried to delay the game until within possible winning range. Because of this, we played two more rounds than expected, even though winner was ultimately not altered.
- Throughout the game, bringing in all 12 men at the same time was mostly useless (excepting the one person who had the legion card) since, unless part of a 5-group, my men just gave opponents an easy target for 1 permanent victory point, so seemed only worth doing very temporarily (i.e. one-time push for a vital point, knowing they'd be sacrificed). I think greater flexibility here would've been far more interesting.
- Battles were rarely tense, usually clear who would win. I know the cards modified this somewhat, but as bonuses developed (e.g. who has creature, who has card bonuses, who has DI cards), the actual gap was usually too significant. In the whole game, there was only one upset (out of easily 30+ battles).

RATING: 8.5
An excellent game - one that prompts a lot of reflection after each game.  Dudes on map with a tech tree, prayer point economy, victory point scoring for satisfying different conditions, and changing turn order too. Plays fast - no wasted time on troop deployments, just charge in, strike and fade.  Different play styles available depending on the tech upgrades (some dynamite combos) Ends in a late game rush, with some chance of king making, so timing and turn order are crucial and positioning is all important.  Reminiscent of Antike in terms of the end game feel.  Great components, art and figures.

RATING: 9.5
[b]2015-11-18 9.5[/b] After 3x 2p 3x 5p:
Typically not a fan of race games (first to score 8 VP's win) but Kemet has a rather special temporary points system. Since permanent points are harder to come by, it is easier to hold back the leader with attacks and at the same time gain aggression points yourself. Absolutely love the deterministic battle cards (DI card draw is random but stronger ones cost prayer points to use) as I quite strongly dislike combat dice. The board cleverly puts every player at equal distance and the power tiles are diverse but balanced. Absolutely love this game and every session does feel different.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 6
Build pyramids, develop knowledge, control temples, move units and fight others for most points.

Plays: 1.
Type: VP race = a game where you race to collect victory points (fastest/most).
Complexity: Medium.
# of players: See BGG.
Playing time: See BGG.
Player interaction: Competition for scarce resources – temples and knowledge. Competition for unlimited resurces - pyramids. Conflict. 
Randomness: Low – event cards, but they are not decisive.

Replayability: Average. It’s one of the quickest civilization games I’ve seen. If you like civ games but don’t like to spend all day, you can still fit in a couple of sessions. I’m not so sure about depth though. As civ games go, this is shallow.
Fun: Average. Improving your faction is fun. Taking developments that give you cool miniatures is fun. 
Memorability: Low. It’s too short to create a story about your civilization. At heart it’s a VP engine.

Thematic integration: Average. The art and presentation does a good job communicating the theme. But it doesn’t feel like the civilization game it wants to be. It feels like a VP engine with a civ theme.
Components: Very cool. This game speaks to my inner 15-year old. The miniatures of different Egyptian-themed creatures are awesome. The game looks great set up. The bits are definitely the best thing about this game. From a playability standpoint I don’t like the player boards, as there’s too much risk of accidentaly bumping the tiny markers.

Who will like this: Teenage boys.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-

RATING: 7.9
Sometimes it’s interesting to play with different groups. I tried Kemet with a group who had played it a couple of times before and it was a bit of a surprise all around. These guys are real ‘engine builders’ whereas I took the approach that as this game rewards aggression (A VP to winners only if they are the attacker) had me attack early and often and try to build up my collection of power tiles to support this strategy. As it turned out I inflicted enough damage on a couple of the other players that their early plans were scrapped and they had to contend with me on the battleground rather than the technology tree thingo. I didn’t win in the end, but did come second and had a ball taking the fight to my opponents.

I enjoyed playing this Ameritrash game and would recommend it to anyone who wants a symmetrical game of conflict where turtling is penalised. The components are pretty nice too; initially I thought the little plastic warriors might be abit too small, but I came to understand they were about the right size for what they needed to do. The constructs are also rather fun.

I would consider purchasing this but would be concerned how much table time it would get.

RATING: 8

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8
Excellent game. Fast and tactical, but the tech trees really add an extra dimension, unlike in some games where they just feel tacked on. The tech upgrades (using the term "tech" loosely since we're talking about mythical creatures, divine powers, etc) are not just an addendum to the game, they're an integral part that's impossible to ignore. 

I also love that the tech upgrades come from a common pool, forcing the players to compete for them, rather than everyone having their own tech tree that they can explore at their leisure. This, combined with the fast pace of the game, makes for an intense fight. You can't just focus on what's happening on the map, nor can you ignore the map and just focus on grabbing tech upgrades. You have to do both simultaneously, and do it quick and decisively lest one of your opponents beats you to the punch. 

I love area control games, even though I'm terrible at them because of my natural tendency to turtle. This game in particular does NOT reward turtling, and that's a good thing. I'd really love to get some more plays of this, to break my turtling habit and eventually maybe even eke out a win.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 6.8
Civilization building, area control, tactical combat, cool minis and an Egyptian theme in a game that can be played in 60 - 90 minutes.  It takes a few gameplays before everything comes together and goes quickly & smoothly.  

2015-06:  Traded it away.  My gaming group never really took to this.  After 6 plays, I felt like it was just OK.  The movement is so abstracted as to be completely non-tactical: you can pretty much get to wherever you need anytime you want.  What this means is that the game  becomes one of building up resources using a dull action selection mechanism and then fighting in an anti-climactic & deterministic combat system.

RATING: 9
After years of play this one is back as my #1 game. I gravitate towards games with very little luck and this satisfies that in a simple system that makes complete sense to me. For those that enjoy combat without virtually any luck I believe this is a must have. 

My cons keeping it from 10 stars is that it has a huge learning curve for a new player. They are flooding with too many technology tiles at once that require reference sheets to understand. I do believe parts of the expansion are must haves that would have been nice in the base game - the extra 2 battle cards alone are crucial to me. The final negative is that I believe that there are a few under-costed or over-costed tiles which limit the amount of variety the game could have had otherwise.

RATING: 4
If you love conflict, this game is for you. If not, stay far, far away. Kemet is a pretty solid combat game, but the problem is, Blood Rage exists. Blood Rage does everything Kemet does in a far better way. There are two major differences between the two. Blood Rage allows you to draft upgrades in a fairly equal manner, while Kemet makes you purchase them. Kemet is also strictly about combat and territory control, while Blood Rage is about getting points. Sometimes that requires conflict, but not always. Both games are effectively two sides of the same coin, but I would much rather play Blood Rage.

RATING: 3
Kemet is a peculiar hybrid of conflict, engine builder and race. While this works OK in games like Scythe, Kemet limits all victory point sources except winning conflicts (and you're able to teleport to virtually any conflict). Hence, what could have been a game of building up a strong power and outmaneuvering your opponents is reduced to build an army, fight a conflict and rebuild an army until someone eventually has enough victories. The fiddly battle system with secret strength cards and multiple modifiers from the technology tree is merely distracting and gives no sense of progress.

RATING: 6.5
When buying, considering get spare red level 4 spare to replace initiative?

Played a two player game which was okay. I think I would like this with more players. We also misplayed with having more than one monster per army which strong just changed the flow of the game, as the snake would go on to cancel all the other monsters effects, which made my strategy ineffective. It was also my first time.

I think the rule book for this game could have explained things a bit more clearly. I also wish the tiles were randomized and only a subset were out because I heard that theres the same general strategies and combos used (like in Caverna). Otherwise, the way combat worked was good, though I wish the numbers on the battle cards had names (like "balanced attack" or "all out offensive" or "a strong defense is a strong offense") and the numbers were a bit more differentiated. It becomes very calculated, tediously checking an opponents strength and potential for damage and such.

What I think I disliked most about the 2p game is that at a certain point, I simply could not win the game. I probably would like it more multiplayer, but I also think I may not enjoy the attacking and destruction as much possibly, as well as semi-limited replayability I expect. Still, its probably one of the best combat game I have ever played, beating out Cyclades and some others. Still not planning to get this, though I may one day. I had also played Suburbia and enjoyed that more today.

Played more after owning and enjoyed a lot more totaling 7 plays. Low luck high strat and high interaxtion make for a fun experience

RATING: 7
This is quite an awesome war game as war games go.  Acquire points by winning fights, buying the correct buildings, owning/conquering temples, and having a 4 score pyramid.  The buildings were the most intriguing part of the game to me, because there was a wealth of interesting tactical and strategic routes you could go.  

My problem with war games is that I don't like to pick on people, and I do not like to be picked on.  Inevitably I will not only lose war games going defensive, but my pathetic retarded feelings get hurt in the meantime.  Ultimately in a war game the 'better' players are going to take advantage of the 'weaker' players and I am not a fan.  I always tend to feel like I was just a ring in the ladder for the winner to step on and over to win, and I don't get much enjoyment out of that in the end.

If I liked war games this would be rated higher, but as it stands this is a solid 7 that I would play again in the future when the mood strikes.

If your group enjoys running in and slapping each other around for a little while before somebody wins, than I can see how this would be a fast (90 min) game for you. However, my gaming group is a euro heavy one, and we analyze all our moves. Which means this game slows down to a crawl. Beautiful components, beautiful artwork, solid gameplay, great depth, but it just continuously overstays its welcome. Perhaps we should try it with less people and see if that works better.

RATING: 8
[b]Kemet[/b] was traded away for Suoer Motherload 6/2/15. 

[COLOR=#FF0000]A year later... thoughts and rating (8):[/COLOR]

[i]I traded this game because it is such a bear to set up and teach, but I would play it anytime. I still think it is a great game, just not a great fit in our small collection. I've completely changed my mind and would rather set up and play Nexus Ops if I want a quick skirmish game and Memoir when we're looking for a longer game. Also if I had one negative I would say that the tiles are a red herring, you really can't get more than a few because you need to be grabbing points and someone else will run away with the game (just ask my son : )[/i]

[COLOR=#FF0000]Initial thoughts and rating (9):[/COLOR]

[i]- Beautiful production quality, especially the minis.
- Love the action selection board where you can only select certain actions once or maybe twice each turn.
- Turns are quick, this game moves along at a brisk pace.
- The pyramids/power tiles in the game add a lot of replay value to the game.
- Creatures have diverse powers and add a lot variety to the battles.
- Divine intervention cards add a layer of unpredictability to the game especially during battles.
- There is no player elimination, you win by having x number of victory points.
- Kemet discourages players from turtling, the majority of victory points are earned by being the attacker in battles. 
- Great combat resolution mechanic with the card system.[/i]

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7.5
心得:
主要是一個互K的遊戲
但是有技能升級
變化性還OK
但是互動主要是在玩家之間的競爭
戰鬥勝利分還是遊戲重點
所以所有可以增加武力的卡都很重要 
除非主城被占領
否則一回合最多戰爭兩次也是一個限制
打不贏的時候就要削弱別人的兵
士兵上限15隻也是一個很大限制
戰爭遊戲而言算是相當不錯的
規則:
*使用對應人數的圖板面，所有力量板塊開著放，寺廟聖域分數放置在上面，其他分數放供應區，DI卡洗勻成牌庫
*每個玩家拿個人板，五個行動標記，1祈禱標記記錄祈禱點數起始5，12個單位，10個放城市自由分配，6戰鬥卡，3金字塔，1DI卡，隨機起始，金字塔分配1/1/1或2/1/0
*金字塔骰只能放在自己的城市，城市內有三區，一區只能放一個
*群聚在同一地點的單位代表軍隊，軍隊上限為五個單位
*祈禱點數上限是11，方形分數是永久，圓形是暫時的
*遊戲每輪分黑夜白天直到有人拿到8分(長遊戲10分)遊戲結束
*第一次玩可每回合開一層的力量板塊，4分遊戲結束
*DI 可在對應時間打出，可能需要付祈禱點數
A 晚上 每人拿兩點祈禱點數(+其他板塊給的) > 拿一張DI卡 (無上限) > 使用力量板塊對應能力 > 分數最低的決定此回合順位(平手由前回合順位前面的決定)
B 白天 每人輪流放一個行動標記到個人圖板對應行動區，並馬上執行，直到放完為止，每個行動區只能放一個，且結束後三層都至少要放一個
1. 拿兩個祈禱點數
2.升級金字塔 付對應等級的祈禱點數，一次只能升級一種，但可升多級，四級金字塔可拿暫時分數 (如果金字塔被別人控制，該玩家獲得所有好處)
3.  買一個力量板塊 金字塔等級必須高於等於該力量板塊，付出等於該等級的祈禱點數，每個人不能擁有兩塊能力一模一樣的板塊
4. 付出祈禱點數拿對應數量的單位放在自己城市的三個區(不管是不是被控制)
5. 移動一個軍隊一步(可移動軍隊內的一部份)，過河算一步，花2點祈禱點數從金字塔傳到方尖碑不算移動點數
*如果要進其它玩家城牆，必須在上回合就在他旁邊
*移動過程中如果跟其他玩加兵在同格就發生戰鬥，進攻跟防守方都選兩張戰鬥卡，第一張棄掉，使用第二張，軍隊數加上卡片力量數加力量板塊或DI卡上的力量數，大的獲勝，平手防守方勝利，兩邊互扣傷害減防禦的兵數，戰鬥卡沒有則把6張在拿回來重用(棄牌可選擇開換蓋)
>進攻方勝利可拿一點永久分數
>失敗如果鄰近有空地可選擇撤退，撤退地點勝利方決定
>戰鬥完可選擇招回，失敗方先，招回的單位回供應區，每一隻加一點祈禱點數
*生物一拿到就可放在任意城市軍隊中，否則沒在場上的要靠徵兵動作上場(一次一隻)(每個軍隊最多一隻生物)，生物不算單位，也不額外加力量，如果軍對消失，生物回手或回城市有兵處
*每輪行動結束後結算神廟，控制delta神廟的可回手一單位拿5祈禱點數，其他神廟拿2/3祈禱點數跟暫時分數，控制聖殿的回手兩單位拿永久1分，空置兩個寺廟的拿1永久分
*結束後分數高的獲勝，平手比戰鬥分，在平手比順位

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 6
I wanted to like it, the concept is good, it just didn't pull it off for me. It's all about the tech's, and what's more it seems to be all about the attack-based techs. Because successful attacks are going to earn you VP's (Nexus Ops style), and even if you buy defense-based techs, you'll still be attacked by attacks with numbers + techs that are mostly guaranteed to win. It just might take them an extra troop or two, and it doesn't help you win. It feels like Game Of Thrones with all the chit based gumpf stripped away. Here, you move, you attack with the similar GoT feeling combat cards, you win, you get a VP. First to 8 or 10 VPs wins. Control 2 or more temples to get an extra VP each turn, giving you a reason to attack a temple space over a normal space. Because you get a VP for winning a battle, it encourages attacking the weakest player unmercifully (to win battles more easily with your weakest battle cards), which is never a good trait for a game to have. I liked how your action board forced you to spread out your 5 actions across the 9 options each turn, so each player does roughly the same things - buy techs, recruit, move/battle each round. Which means that once the good tech combos are bought, imposing favourites and non-favourites, it feels like it comes down to who gets picked on the most and who gets picked on the least relative to the perceived strength of the combos. Which introduces a leader-hitting whine-fest. Which is not that appealing to me in the main.

RATING: 10
Beautiful pieces and superbly designed. The game is deep, balanced, fast-paced, easy to learn and get into (though it's heavier than the average game), allows lots of player interaction while still leaving much space for individual engine building. It's a perfect hybrid of a dudes-on-a-map wargame with an economic technology building Eurogame. A relatively quick, tense and fascinating game that's still challenging after a few games because there are many paths to victory and it's all about doing what's best on the board with the players around you and their strategies.
BUT partly because it's so brilliantly done - not so epic.

RATING: 2
This game makes me sad. It makes no difference what you do, there is no interaction or plays to be made, no excitement. You can come up with an intricate plan and follow it, or you can do what you want every turn and it will make no difference. Every available action is balanced specifically to be mathematically equal to any other action without any interactive potential. Optimization strategies are negated by the forced caps on army and prayer.
Kemet also boasts a hilarious amount of redundancies, both in components and mechanics. To list just a few issues:
- Many different kinds of tokens because they didn't bother to design and elegant vp system (it's a multi-step operation at the end of every round)
- Ridiculously oversized map
- Pyramid dice which, while thematic, are both unnecessary and ugly
- Poorly designed low-cap resource system
- There are only 12 units per player, they're identical, and they're used to act out the game's version of military actions in a highly predictable and dry manner

RATING: 10

RATING: 7
3 plays - Overall fun, but not all that strategically deep. It is a very streamlined "dudes on a map" experience. The game places various restrictions on the players with limit your strategic options. For example, how many total troops can be recruited and limiting the types of actions players can take. This makes players moves somewhat predictable. 

The strategic elements are in what card(s) you play in battle and how you build up a repotoire of rulebreaking/ power enhancing tiles (either abilities or unique troops). These are probably the most fun part and give you a sense of 'army building' and 'evolving into something unique'. However, you need to layout like 16 tiles for each themed technology (48 or so total). I really dislike games that have this 'mess' around the board and are difficult to place so all players can see. Player aids are much needed, so you can just keep the tiles in a stack near the board and select from a much more player friendly 'menu'. The number of tiles is obviously not new-player friendly either. 

The streamlined gameplay ultimately makes it feel like there's not a lot of depth to your choices, expansion or overall strategy... The game swings quickly, so it doesn't necessarily reward consistently good play, but rather late game swings/ victories. 
While this is probably more fun than I've described (as indicated by rating), there are loads of good area control games I'd rather play aka mare nostrum, chaos in the old world, cthulhu wars, Forbidden Stars, Eclipse, scythe, mission red planet and more. 

Also, this is not a 2p game.

Fun, but doesn't give me the "dudes on a map" experience I'm looking for.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7
This is a multiplayer wargame with simple combat rules and a large technology track.  Like many recent games of its ilk, Kemet strongly rewards combat with victory points for the victor, which makes the "turtling" strategy untenable.  The technology system is a little difficult to wrap your mind around, as most techs are available early, and you can only find out what they do by reading a booklet.  It's a lot to digest.  However, they do provide some nice differentiation among players (though it's somewhat frustrating to find out the tech you wanted is already taken, so you can't get it).  The board is cleverly designed so that players are roughly equidistant--you aren't really closer to your apparent "neighbors".  The game is definitely more involved than something like Nexus Ops, but I'd say a "more involved Nexus Ops" might be a good comparison.

(1 play)

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 10
fter about 6 months spent in painting miniatures for creatures and units (on our free time, obviously not full time), organizing sometimes full week-ends in order to keep on painting, we eventually finished and started playing... and it's great! Hallelujah to Matagot and its authors for it!

I've played games at 2 and 5 people, quite different but both nice. At 2 players you can afford to develop a counter-strategy, even in defense, which is also nice. Although of course a good defense is sometimes a good offense. 
At 5 players the game is both longer (we ended up at 3hours with 3 new players) and more chaotic. However you still need to have a strategy if you wanna take the uphold hand at some point and try to keep it. But you also have to be able to adapt a lot, a there's only so much tiles available, and sharing between 5 limits your possibilities pretty fast (The Ta-Seti expansion is quite appreciated for addressing that issue).

The rules are clear, but I confess I spent some time on BGG looking for details or questions that were not addressed in the rulebook, although it always made sense.

Although it was a first, the new player got it very fast and teleportation helping, you get into action pretty fast (after all it is a fight game). 

I love how you can pump up your strength and resources (prayer points) through the tiles, you end up building pretty nice combos that bring a true difference to the battle outcome. 
The creatures speak for themselves, and you always bring a little anxiety to other players when you come to conquer their territory with a giant scorpion!

All in all it's a fantastic game, both pretty simple to get the basis and rich enough so that you can change and adapt strategies a lot. I also recommend the Ta-Seti expansion to add some nice new features to the game, although making it longer.

RATING: 10
One of the best war game of all time !! A little bit similar to Cyclades, but different in so many ways as this game favours aggressive offensive style.

The depth of this game resides in building and upgrading your army and fight for the Victory. Infinite possibilities on how you want to customize your army: defense, offense, balanced... you decide how you want to go to war & win !!

This is a gamers game. That's the main difference from Cyclades as it's more appealing to casual gamers.

If you are a wargame fan, this is a must.

RATING: 7
Interesting game where your job is to attack everyone everywhere all the time. You also don't lose a lot of game progress if you die or are defeated in combat, which is refreshing. The minis are beautiful and the three tech trees you can invest into are very well thought out.

There is an ability for players to take control of other player's homes, although there is a lot standing in their way to do so. I'm tempted to say that the whole thing is unnecessary, and if they wanted a way for players to aggressively benefit from each others pyramid progress, there could be a better way of doing that so you could ignore the whole 'doesn't need to stop for walls' thing. it is fun punting back and forth in and out of temples and victory point areas. It's not fun when your engine gets shut down by a player standing on your house rather than benefiting himself more by standing on points in the field. Again, this eventuality is rare. I've only had it happen to me once, and it ended with the player not involved in that exchange winning, so there you go. Still, why even have it?

:star: Art, Iconography and components functional
:star: Clever or interesting mechanisms
:star: Reasonably low downtime between turns with mildly AP players
:star: Multiple plays stay interesting
:star: Low or mitigatable luck elements
:star::star::nostar::nostar::nostar: Raw enjoyment
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8.5
It may look like a war game.  It may sound like a war game.  But Kemet is not a war game.  Sure, there's troops.  Yes, there's combat, of sorts.  But don't let that fool you.  Once you get your head around the somewhat arcane iconography, you'll find a deep, rich, tight, cutthroat tactical Euro.  
When playing Kemet, I am, strangely, most reminded of a game like Claustrophobia.  It is a wonderful hybrid of all the things I like about Ameritrash games (cool mini's, variable powers, etc.) blended with the elegance you usually find in your best Euros.  It plays quickly, and is deeply satisfying.  
Absolute recommendation.

RATING: 6
A big, ambitious, messy dudes-on-a-map game that just doesn't quite come together. There is a huge number of technology tiles, and you need to understand most or all of them in order to form a strategy and figure out what your opponents are going for. This is not helped by the mediocre iconography and lack of text on the tiles, so players need to constantly consult and pass around the manual just to figure out what their options are. The end condition of getting 8 victory points feels unsatisfying, and the fact that victory points are somewhat scarce causes issues too. Since even a swing of 1 or 2 points is very significant in the last few turns, there is a lot of bashing the leader as everyone tries to prevent whoever is currently in the lead from winning, rather than try to win themselves. This makes the game feel like it comes down too much to the turn order, in a way that can't really be planned for, so the victory seems random and somewhat unearned. The odd movement system feels like it bogs down the game, where you can teleport troops away from their starting location, but after that they move so slowly that it's often not worth bothering with. I almost wanted my troops to die sometimes so I could re-summon them in my base and teleport them out again.
The deterministic card combat is nice though, and gives a nice bit of strategy.

RATING: 5
Kemet is... fine.
It's well balanced, tactically thoughtful, and the technology upgrade system is clever and nuanced.

It also wasn't very exciting.

It's easy to learn, difficult to completely master, and offers enough strategic options that you could try something new every time.
Mechanically there's nothing I can point out as being unpolished or troublesome.

But I never felt those tense moments of snatching victory from the jaws of defeat, or pride in the empire I had built.  
Somehow the spirit of fun just didn't manifest itself within Kemet's structure, and I'm not sure why.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7
This is an Ameritrash game  with a lot of Euro mechanisms. Your main objective is to attack other players. The map is very homogeneous, such that there is no way to put your troops in clever positions in order to defend an area, especially  since you can easily teleport to relevant locations (temples, which give you VP). You just stay  at temples or attack opponents at temples. 

I really enjoy combat, in which you use your deck of cards (mostly identical for every player) instead of dice. Especially since winning (for VP) and loosing troops are  two different aspects of fighting. There you can really out-think your opponent. 

The game really shines with all the different power tiles you can purchase and especially the giant creatures. You can really try different strategies and I do not see a completely overpowered tile (Initiative may be nasty to play against, but this does not generate so much VP).

The end game is a bit of a let down, since the being last in turn order in the later (or last) turn is a huge advantage. Very often the leader is bashed, such that there is an additional round, in which the last player very  often can score some easy VP (at empty temples) and gain victory. This in combination with divine intervention cards makes  me consider  Kemet an Ameritrash game with Euro mechanics (rather than the other way round). In future games I need to  adjust my play style and play faster, since there is some danger for AP prone players. Unfortunately, Kemet takes some time to explain and is not easy to get to table with new players because of all the power tiles and divine intervention cards.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 9.5
Easily one of my favorite games.  I love how tight the game is and how it is usually decided on the last turn who the game victory will swing to.  All decisions are meaningful and it is pretty balanced and and plays quickly across all player counts.

RATING: 8
It's a pleasant surprise to come across a guys-on-a-map game that deftly sidesteps most of the pitfalls of the genre. It's pacy, there's little reason to gang up, and if you turtle you lose. And it doesn't overstay its welcome by a minute. The mechanics are stripped down to a purring and compact engine, and then built up with gorgeous accessories. The special abilities, creatures, and artwork really bring the game to life. Kemet knows exactly what kind of game it wants to be and nails it.

It does seem susceptible to kingmaking, though. In our first game I was in a position where two other players would each win with 10 VP immediately after my turn. I could strike and potentially take a temple from both of them. I chose the one whose army was somewhat weaker, in order to improve my own chances of gaining points. Still, it felt kind of cheesy.

RATING: 7
There's an awful lot to like in this game. I like that it's almost all about fighting, but with not a single die to be seen (well, OK, the pyramids look like outsized D4s, but aren't used for that...) - there's actually very little luck in the game (only in which Divine Intervention cards you draw), but lots to think about. The combination of "permanent" and "temporary" victory points also leads to interesting decisions. I don't think this is a classic, and I rarely find myself actively desiring to play it (I haven't played this since 2013, actually), but it's certainly a very good game, and fairly unique too.

It's also extremely pretty and with some excellent miniatures for the many creatures. Perhaps my rating of 7 is too low, but despite all I find to praise in this game, it is not one that I would ever feel like specifically requesting.

RATING: 8.5
Kemet is a king of the hill, dudes-on-a-map game with heavy euro elements. Kemet provides several different sources of victory points and a lot of options for customizing your armies to suit. Since only one type of action token is used for all types of actions (managing resources, specialising your army and maneuvering your army) players are constantly torn between what to do. The game encourages attacking and makes it easy to recover lost units. Highly recommended for those looking for a strategic dudes-on-a-map game.

RATING: 8.25

I like the Powers, basically a tech tree, and the combat cards work well.  This is just a great tactical combat game that plays well and fairly quickly.

RATING: 5
Everyone is encouraged to go whacking someone else in this reasonably quick game of "Let's go whacking". And it does it reasonably well. Everything is within whacking distance. There's upgrades to improve your whacking abilities or to increase your income to buy more things with greater whacking. All in all it's a fun enough romp. Pick up some complementary abilities. Don't overextend yourself. Whack the leader and encourage others to do the same… even if you are the leader, because being whacked can be fun (and profitable) too. It's not bad. Simple enough to dive into after an extended break. Short enough that it doesn't outstay its welcome. Not very highbrow, but it's got nice bits and should be fun with the right group. So only whack the kids when playing Kemet - it's the only time whacking is cool.

RATING: 4
I love some of the ideas that went into this game, but what I'm increasingly realizing is that you should virtually never leave guys outside of your city. Taking over temples is a trap. That just invites someone to attack you, and if they win the combat, they get a point. My last game I made numerous attacks, but I virtually always recalled my guys afterwards. It just wasn't worth the possibility of getting attacked.

It's impossible to lock down a region and maintain control of it. This inability to maintain control of an area and the lack of strong defensive options really hurt this game, and the way that everyone can easily gang up on the leader makes the endgame way too gamey, king makery, and chaotic.

I like the tech tiles, and the way that the different factions differentiate themselves over time through the techs, but I think some of the bonuses are too similar.

RATING: 7
Whoa, the designers went ahead and addressed every single issue people have with a dudes-on-a-map game (DoaM)!

 - Playtime is short, only need 2 hours, tops
 - 1 action per player (instead of 1 massive turn per player) to keep players engaged and reduce downtime
 - Elimination of the 4-player adjacency problem by allowing for teleportation and dynamic military situations a la Rex and (to a lesser extent) Dune
 - Attacking is heavily incentivized (permanent victory points)
 - No bash-the-weak syndrome and no doggy-piling because of the novel 'Recall' idea, allowing you to melt your soldiers down for money and focus elsewhere instead of being a wounded duck inviting a doggy pile after a battle (damned if you win, damned if you lose). Virtually impossible to be ganged up on.
 - Nice benefit for the last-place player (rigging the turn order). I can see people sandbagging and trying to be last, then aiming for the ultimate push a la Power Grid and TI3.

Each of the Euro-mechanisms is thoughtfully integrated to smooth out the rough edges. Unlike Cyclades, which shoehorns an auction in for no good reason.

But is it any fun? Too early to say for me now. I do have a few quibbles:

 - Very conservative with the power level of techs and cards. Hopefully an expansion will unleash the nukes, similar to how Cyclades: Hades was a lethal adrenaline shot.
 - Don't quite like the same techs appearing every time, and having to come in with pre-thought out tech orders. I'm sure the first thing the expansion would do is add more tech options.
 - Some player asymmetry would be nice.
 - (Biggest issue) The 'map' in DoaM feels more like a grid. Very few areas and not too hard to make it so you can reach any space you want.
 - Component-wise, the board is way bigger than it needs to be (or the soldiers are way smaller than what they could be), which is nice for aesthetics, but makes the game take up a lot of unnecessary space since there's a lot of off-board components.

The good more than outweighs the bad right now. Especially the prospect of a satisfying DoaM game that supports up to 5 clocking in at under 2 hours.

[hr]

Lowered design to 4. 4 issues:
 - the board/grid issue is annoying - noted above
 - better to have a subset of techs instead of the same 48 techs every time (think Puerto Rico expansion) - noted above
 - the techs also need to scale with # of players. Weird with 3er and everybody getting a guaranteed VP + silver disc
 - feels more like a battle between prehistoric alien races when playing this instead of history/myth. Mythological creatures are par for the course but the teleportation is too much of a logical leap. Should have used an alien theme with stargates.

4+3

RATING: 7
FINALLY got a 4-player game in with the crew.  I have to say, while I like the fundamental nature of Kemet (in-your-face area control, vying for VP's, upgrades, etc.), I wasn't as impressed as I'd had hoped.  The "race to 8" seems cool except that it really enables hard-core leader bashing, and your group has to be OK with that kind of experience (my group is 100% ok with it).  The huge array of techs available right from the beginning is inelegant and overwhelming.  On the plus side, all of us felt there were some really cool tech-combos/strategies you could pursue in this game, once you became familiar with it.  

There's not much fun tactical movement-maneuvering going on (unless you have a monster that boosts your movement, and even then...).  Perhaps most damning, the combat is surprisingly dry.  The variety in battle cards is limited, and the "discard 1, use 1" mechanic seemed unnecessary to me (why not force players to use all 6 before getting them back?).  I just felt like we never got excited about resolving those cards.  TBH, I'd rather play Clockwork Wars...

RATING: 2
One of my major recent disappointments. 

It's an euro-ameritrash hybrid all the way, or rather the game has two distinct halves. The ameritrash half is the best kind of DoaM (awarding attacking, map makes it easy to reach objectives). The eurogame half is my most hated type of euro - you have to put together an engine with few parts and limited actions (everything I hated in Endeavour). You just have to internalise this weird contraption of a tech three. Blargh. The problem with this hybrid is that previous well known hybrids were inspired by simplicity and streamlined euro approach, while this one embraces the solitarish puzzle aspect which recently dominates in euro sphere. Also why would combat cards be better than dice is a question only FFG knows the answer for.

Difference to Cyclades - Cylades' euro part (auction) is very interactive and should be played aggressively, Kemet's euro part is a puzzle against the game (okay, you can grab pieces before other players take them, whoop dee doo). Also people I played it with complained about king making - this rarely happens in Cyclades as there people watch over each other much closer and win conditions are simpler to keep a track off.  


Bottom line: I'll avoid this one and hope it doesn't have offspring. It also takes a very peculiar type of gamer: one who likes both attacking and optimising (what happened to my hobby?!). The group I played this with then moved to CitOW (whoo hoo!).

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 6
[2014-07-12]
Óriási várakozással ültem le a játékhoz, mert nagyon felkapott és mindenhol dicsért játékról van szó. Ez egy full konfrontatív harci játék, ami gyakorlott játékosokkal tényleg rövid játékidejű lehet. Összesen 12 egységünk lehet, amivel Egyiptomban harcolunk egymással. Templomokat és egymás piramisait próbáljuk elfoglalni. Egy-egy csatáért a győztesnek jár győzelmi pont. A csatákat fix támadó lapokkal segítjük. Összehasonlítjuk a támadóerőt és kiszámoljuk a veszteségeket. Én magam részéről ezt még egyszerűbben oldottam volna meg, de nem rossz ez a része a játéknak. Számomra a legjobban tetsző dolog az akciók menedzselése. Egyrészt vannak imapontok, amik tulajdonképpen a pénzt jelentik a játékban, másrészt van alapesetben 5 akciónk egy körben. Ezt 7 fajta akcióra tudjuk elkölteni, amik közül van 2 egyforma is. Ezek egy piramisra vannak rajzolva 2-3-4 eloszlásban, amihez a szabály, hogy minden sorban kell egy akciót választanunk. Ez ötletes és jól működő dolog. Ezen túl még 3x16 lapka közül tudunk magunknak vásárolni, amik között van ugyan egyforma, de így is rengeteg lehetőséget adnak és különböző stratégiai utat a játékhoz. Ezek a lapok különböző dolgokban segíthetnek minket a játék során. Megszerzésükhöz pénzre van szükségünk, illetve megfelelő szintű és színű piramisra a főhadiszállásunkon. Eddig csak pozitívumot írtam a játékról, most jöjjenek a negatívumok. A kártyák, a lapkák, a saját tablók rettentő szépek, jól kidolgozottak, de valaki más csinálhatta a játék tábláját, mert az hihetetlen rondára sikerült valamiért. A győzelmi pontok sokféle jelölővel kerülnek kiosztásra, amik figyelése rettentő macerás. Felróható a Kemetnek a holtidő. Igaz, öten játszottunk, valószínűleg kevesebben már sokkal kevésbé lehet zavaró, de egy-egy lépésen lehet azért gondolkodni. A játék legnagyobb hibája azonban a királycsinálás. Szinte bele van kódolva a társasba ez. Kiesni nem lehet ugyan a játékból, de simán le lehet verni valakit annyira, hogy semmi esélye se legyen a játékban. Nem lehet benne olyan stratégiát kitalálni, amit két játékos ne tudna tönkretenni, ha ez a célja. Én úgy gondolom bárki is nyer meg egy Kemetet, ahhoz szüksége van arra, hogy ne figyeljenek rá vagy esetleg direkt ne üssék. Így a játék csak akkor fog jó játékélményt adni, ha ugyanolyan szintű és ezt megértő játékosok játsszák, más esetben biztos lesz olyan, aki rossz szájízzel áll föl a játéktól. Csalódás volt a Kemet, pontosabban egyértelműen nem az én játékom. A konfliktusos játékot kedvelőknek biztosan tetszeni fog, mert sok egyedi ötlet van benne és ha ki tudtak volna valamit találni a királycsinálásra, akár még jó játék is lehetett volna.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 6
Unlike Cyclades, you actually get to use the cool monster sculpts, yea!

Like Cyclades, seems like it comes down to weird turn order/kingmaking issues in the late game. In newbie games, most games will probably be decided by players leaving weak armies around to pick off (attacking a weak army is the easiest way to get points, so it's important not to leave easy kills around). In experienced games I think it'll be turn order abuse.

The funnest part of this game is all in the tech combos and competing over assembling yours while other players are taking them. And that novelty wears off. The rest of the game can be a little boring. 

Also attacking is so beneficial that the game can seem to end up having a turtley feel, ironically -- you go after surefire or almost surefire wins and melt your dudes down for money, leaving as few attackable targets as possible until someone gets such a badass combo going that you have to stop them. Or you can make a surprise movement/breach and take someone unaware despite their turtling.

This game seems to be often compared to Chaos in the Old World and Cyclades for whatever reason. Not sure I think the comparison to CitOW is valid, but if we're doing that then my take is Chaos in the Old World > Cyclades > Kemet. But personally I'd sooner compare it to Clash of Cultures and Starcraft, and it loses there as well.

[b]I'd Rather Play:[/b] Forbidden Stars, Starcraft, Clash of Cultures

(4 Design + 2 Enjoyment)

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8
This game is amazing in so many ways but I don't think it has staying power.

First of all, the game is reasonably easy to teach and play and can be finished in about an hour.  For a game that accessible and reasonably paced the components are pretty outstanding.  Each army has a different color and design, the cardboard tiles are thick and sturdy, the art work is all pretty, and each of the mythical animals has a great looking plastic miniature.

The game is a reasonably light combat/area control game played very similarly to A Game of Thrones.  That being that combat is resolved by playing cards with different attack/casualty/protection values on them.  Attacking is the easiest, fastest, and most efficient way to make points but there are other very viable methods.  Control of certain temple areas can also gain you points, a special temple allows you to sacrifice units for points, and many of the higher level powers come with points.

Our first game ended with a pretty close spread, 10/10/9, and we all pursued pretty different strategies so they are all certainly viable.  I look forward to playing the game again to try different approaches.

That all said after one play I've seen most of what this game has to offer.  The map is pretty small, there are only 16 different powers available in 3 different colors and they're all visible/available from the start of the game, and the only other variable factor are these Divine Intervention cards that really just give basic combat and action bonuses.

I see the game getting very play-by-numbers after just a few plays which is disappointing for such a high quality game that also fits an accessible and timely game space.

After playing a game with a full compliment of players it is worth mentioning that the game feels very different.  There's a lot more competition for the powers.  With 3 players we tended to each focus on a different color and usually could get whatever we wanted first.  With 5 you will most likely have competition with others over a color you focus on.  There's also a lot more down time which can get a little bothersome.

RATING: 6
I got to play this and didn't like it when I really expected to love it. I do want to play again to make sure it wasn't just an oddity. But I have had the same feeling with the designers' other game, Cyclades.

The way the technology system works makes it to much of a race to specific powers. A player that doesn't get the power he was going for can be very hampered. Many of the powers are out of balance for their power level. This appears to have been designed this way to make the creatures more cool. If one player is going after a path that others are not, he will be at a huge advantage while the other players compete over the powers.

The combat system also didn't sit well with me. I love the similar system found in A Game of Thrones 2nd ed. The difference in aGoT is the combat cards are not the same for every player in combat, attrition and powers. With Kemet, the cards are identical for all players in the combat and attrition. Players will almost always play the best card for attack unless out numbered. If out numbered they will almost always play the best card for attrition. The variation in combat is done with randomly drawn cards that may or may not even be cards to use in combat. This system feels even more random and cheep than using dice. At least with dice you have some probability to go off of.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7.5
Good lightweight Wargame. Offers a 'customized strike team' experience.

Game has three main parts:
1 worker placement mechanic, however you are always guaranteed your actions and also have enough workers at your disposal. What this does is that the focus shifts more to a 'when' will you execute your actions as opposed to 'can' you take those actions, as in many euros. The only restricted area is the tile-taking and that means everyone is inclined to take such actions first, thereby telegraphing a lot of their tactics so you can react to this. This keeps each turn interesting as you have to pay attention to what others do.

2 The tile buying mechanic. This customizes your army and economic engine with unique capabilities only you possess in that game. As they are gradually bought, it allows everyone to keep track of their opponents power curve, both economic as combat strength wise. This is the hearth of the game, which explains its appeal to the non-Wargamer crowd.

3 Finally there's combat. Which is a simple affair of dropping your biggest stack where it needs to be, select a card from an ientical, limited and diminishing stack of modifier cards and resolve both asualties and outcome. Which are interestingly not coupled. You might win a battle while losing more figures. This, coupled with the short distance, the abundance of mobility and the cap on a maximum number of figures in an area (which is easily reached) makes warfare a non-strategic 'stack dropping' experience which focuses on quick point grabbing.

The last point reminiscences the way Dune works, which helps to keep the game tense. It is about grabbing victory, not grinding your way to victory.

All in all quite an interesting game, wonder if no dominant strategy will appear as there is not much hidden information in this game (as opposed to the classic Dune). 

Final huge benefit it brings: playable in one evening. Which often is a stretch for some other contenders in this genre (a game of thrones and chaos in the old world).

Disadvantage? You'll need minimum four but preferably five players for it to work. Not that interesting with 2 or three. 

Good fast light wargame, recommended.

RATING: 4
Much like Cyclades, this is a battle game with special powers. Unlike Cyclades, however, Kemet has a lot of special powers, and I mean a lot. Truth be told, the board's topology is almost a non-issue. With everything being so close together, and the ability to "teleport" to the handful of locations farther away, everyone can launch an attack against everyone. Your real choice is what cards to purchase, and how fast to purchase them. I'd almost rather play this game as a card game and be done with all the fancy plastic miniatures and huge board. They aren't doing very much work anyway.

The battle system is interesting, but ultimately a bit tedious. It reminds me of the combat system from Dune (now Rex), but less elegant. Or maybe a better comparison is Game of Thrones. The battle cards are a neat idea, but I can't help but think there's a better way to handle bluff.

It's a fine game for what it is, and if you're into special powers, combo-building, and tit-for-tat combat, you'll like Kemet. For me, I'll pass. Finally, it's worth mentioning that the game has big potential for king-making scenarios, so it's best to play at least a little fast and loose.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-

Much more straight forward than Cyclades, less random, more chaos, and a lot more attacking. Probably less strategic, but slightly more fun. Still amazing game :)

RATING: 9
This game really hits a sweet spot for me and left an amazing first impression. It's a hybrid between conflict-driven game and Euro game with an emphasis on the conflict, because in the end the engine you're building aims at nothing else but optimizing the conflicts you are bound to have. And what's even more incredible is the fact that the game achieves to leave this fantastic impression even as a 3-player game. This is usually where any conflict-heavy game struggles. "Lurk but don't be the first one to attack or be attacked!": That's the golden rule in a 3-player game. If you happen to be the party lucky enough not to be involved in the first brawl, you'll be in an advantageous position. Here, the limited amount of troops and the possibility to recall the troops for both the attacker and defender make for situations with lots of back and forth with all three parties involved. Really excellent, extremely well balanced game!

RATING: 7
Not a bad game, and I like it much better than the often-compared Cyclades.

Initially I was excited for this game because it seemed like a meatier version of Nexus Ops, which is probably my favorite game in this genre. After playing Kemet, though, it feels more like Eclipse. This is due largely to the way the turns work. In Nexus Ops you move your whole army and take your whole turn at once. In Kemet and Eclipse, you take one action at a time. This results in making subtle moves and trying to anticipate what your opponents will do.

I'm giving this a 7 for now because the large number of power tiles are a lot to take in. I anticipate this rating going up after becoming more familiar with the game and the various tiles.

Sold.

RATING: 9
I'm giving it an 8 right away for being a fairly streamlined conflict game. I want to own this some day and have it right next to Chaos in the Old World. I imagine this one would actually get more plays because it's easier to teach, plays quicker, and has a more presentable theme.

::UPDATE:: 

8/28/13 - Boy was I right. With only one more play, this game jumped up to my #2 spot in my collection. It's tense, it's interesting, it's tight, it's simple, it's, it's, it's everything a Euro fanatic wants in a conflict game. The way the system makes interesting decisions from the tech upgrades, and all the possible paths to victory is nothing short of brilliance. Attacking is balanced--not too swingy like dice can be. 

I'm going to write up a more proper assessment of why I love it so much, but for now suffice it to say that it does so much right that it has the potential to be one of my favorite games of all time. The only thing left to test it is to see whether or not it stands up to a lot of plays without starting to get dry or repetitive. 

Highly recommended. 

::UPDATE::

8/28/13 - Some comments I made elsewhere to sum up my feelings about Kemet:

Far and away, the most memorable and enjoyable game session I've had during the last 6 months came from a 5-player game of [b][thing=127023]Kemet[/thing][/b] this past Sunday night with one of my nieces and 3 of my nephews.

Such a fantastic, tense, and interesting game.

What I love about this game is how much fun you can have building up an attack engine (or not) and discovering your own way to build your VPs. There are so many possibilities in the strategic choices. It's just combo, and engine-building bliss. Kemet is by far my favorite hybrid. It's climbing way up in my rankings. It will definitely be top 5. I haven't decided exactly how high it will go, but I'm thinking possibly #3. And that's with only two plays under my belt. 

::UPDATE::

It is currently my #2 ranked game of all time with a 9.5 rating.

RATING: 7.5
The theme is what instantly sold me on this game. Ancient Egypt with gods and monsters thrown in the mix. The players all start as equals but that quickly changes even as fast as during the set-up of the game. Choosing which powers to go for and which abilities to invest in is one of the strategic decisions to this game. I was amazed to see that each different faction, representing a different Egyptian god, has a different look and theme. A minor detail that makes armies feel different.

The one downside is that the power tiles take up a LOT of space. I've solved this by using plastic sheets to store coins to store individual tiles. This makes it easier for all the players to look at the available tiles. 

No matter the player count, this is a good, thematic game. Sadly, Blood Rage has surpassed this game for me and my group, so I don't play it as often. 
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7.5
[thing=127023][/thing] is a game of area control and conflict - high amounts of conflict - with an Egyptian theme. The players are Egyptian Gods (Bastet, Anubis, Ouadjet, Sobek and others) that uses their powers to be the biggest of them all.

The players are trying to have 8 (short game) or 10 (standard) victory points by the end of a given round in order to win. Points come from: winning a battle as the attacker, controling temples, making sacrifices at the Temple of All Gods, developing pyramids to their highest level, acquiring blessings, and so on. Some of these are temporary points (temples, pyramids), that others can take from you, others you won't lose (battles, sacrifices).

The game has some similarities with [thing=103343][/thing]: the way the game ends by points, how you can lose them if you lose a certain place or thing, and how the battles takes place: each troop is worth a point, then you must chose two battle cards from your hand, one do discard, one to use. The cards adds strenght to your troops, and can also cause damage and provide protection - the same things you can get with the House cards. Also, you only get the cards used/discard when you cycle your whole hand of cards. But I like that [thing=127023][/thing] has permanent points instead of a limit of time. With the permanent points the game is sure to reach a place where is impossible to prevent a win - the victory can change hands, but the game will end no matter who takes it. With only temporary points (as in [thing=103343][/thing]) you can be facing two or more players and go from 6 to 1 or 2 points. In [thing=127023][/thing] you can be beat in the same way, but you would go from 6, to say, 4 points. This is a huge difference in the "I'm still in the game" front.

[thing=127023][/thing] has some really nice components, especially the miniatures (even the armies are all different one from another, not only apart in color). The game is easy to teach and to play - most parts are intuitive enough (we understood the players boards without explanation, for instance). The manual is lacking more explanation about the use of the tiles and the FAQ for [thing=127023][/thing] is not really a little one (as I have been said, I don't actually see it) - but the game owner make sure to say that is paramount to read the FAQ before playing.

One play took about 100 minutes in the short game (8 victory points). I can't say I agree that the standard game will take only 90 minutes, but it is possible, I guess.

I liked the game well enough and I'm willing to play it again, given the opportunity (as I don't own the game). So, [thing=127023][/thing] is not only a pretty face.

RATING: 8
A very enjoyable hybrid style game. So far it seems well balanced, with a possible minuscule disadvantage to the left side of the board with five players (because the game favors attacking and three players are on the right, near the closest temple, only two on the left). There are multiple paths to victory, but winning battles seems to play an important part in every game I have played to date. Power tiles are important but there are several paths to gaining an edge in battle (meaning that if one player takes the most desired tiles, there are useful alternatives). Good power tiles are harder to get the more players added...and it really sucks for the player who is trying to level up their pyramid to get something only to be beat out by an opponent and have to start over and take a new route to victory. Luckily, the variation on turns partly solves this dilemma (if one knows what they are doing)...however, be aware that it isn't always advantageous to go first, and it isn't necessarily a disadvantage to go last. Everything must be carefully weighed. A great game. So glad I added it to my collection.

RATING: 7

Unfortunately, there are so many of them that you will need to print a quick summary of all of them and accept that new players will need a moment for themselves browsing them. No need to own this game, but would be willing to play again.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8.5
Kemet is an excellent game.

Although it's kind of the sequel to Cyclades, it uses a ton of different mechanics and really comes out to be a different game. To the point that nobody should be worried about getting both because they are both excellent and not the same.

On to Kemet. First off, Kemet has beautiful components. The miniatures, while most of them are small, are really cool looking. Each player gets a customized set of miniatures. There are also creature miniatures that you can recruit the help of and these are incredibly awesome. If only I had the artistic talent to paint them. The game uses a bit of the command and colors scheme (with attack cards) while also a bit of worker placement to take certain actions. Uses technology tree as well. I guess I could call this a very very light 4X game. It definitely encourages attacking and movement around the board. Most wargames are really slow to start, but Kemet gets right into the action.

So much to say here, but I need to play more to really start getting into the strategy aspects of it. 

Overall, Kemet is a fantastic game that wraps a bunch of heavy concepts into a nice tight package.

RATING: 7
Simple rules, beautiful components. I didn't really learn the symbolics yet and think it could have been done better. RftG does symbols right(how sick that might sound), Kemet could have learned a bit more from that.

Gamewise it looked at first like a game of Risk, but quickly it became clear that this has a lot more going on. I especially like the technology tree system that I wish to see in other games as well. Choices made have big impact on your game so winning feels like pure skill and no luck. Kingmaking is less of a problem than in other similar games. Playtime also feels like it hits the right spot. Epic feel is a downside as there is no feel of distance, all the temples are practically one teleport away which makes the game quite a tight and even struggle. Some technologies felt like they were way too powerful.
I guess my main problem is that I just don't like it, but still it's a high rating because there is so much good in the game. I guess my hating might be more about genre than the game itself.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 9
Very good, potentially great with more plays.

Archetypical Eurotrash game. Action pyramid where each action can only be chosen once each round and actions must be balanced across the levels of the pyramid. Engine-building with special power tiles which give improved and new abilities. Non-random combat for resources and victory points. Lots of direct conflict, but resources and actions need to be managed carefully. Great components.

This is very much a Eurotrash game - and thus cursed. With a Euro front-end and an Ameritrash back-end there is something that most people will probably not like much or even dislike.

But for us broad-minded connoisseurs of fine gaming who appreciate the complex nuances of interwoven levels and styles of game play it is great.

A bit complex and slow for new players as you need/want to study the many different special power tiles, but not too bad - 2.5 hours for 5 first-timers including rules explanation.

ESSENTIAL: Print a copy of the Divine Intervention cards and Power Tiles index for all players - and lay out the power tiles on the table in the same order as on the index, so that it is easy to see which power tiles have already been taken and which are still available. This will probably reduce your playing time significantly.

RATING: 3
While the two games are vaguely similar, Kemet does everything wrong that Cyclades does right. Cyclades has an interesting auction that really is the heart of the game, while in Kemet they decided to keep the action selection simple and make combat much more complicated with several decks of cards and oodles of special power tiles. The result is jarring: a game that is massively more complex yet for my tastes less interesting as there is little subtlety to the combat system, just +x modifiers left and right for your hit points, defense points, wound points, etc... you get the idea.

The auction in Cyclades was a focal point of interaction; Kemet with its smorgasbord of special powers is much more self-absorbed and consequently much less interactive (constantly we were forgetting whose turn it was, which would never happen in Cyclades). Fans of multi-player conflict games with shiny components and lots of chrome that don't mind shallow game play will be perfectly at home here. I would rather play a myriad of other multi-player conflict games that aren't as overwrought and actually have interesting decision-spaces.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7
Wow. What a gorgeous game. Everything from the map to the ink washed miniatures just drips with its mythical Egyptian theme.

The game itself is slightly one dimensional, but it works within that confined space very, very well. There's no engine to build, no objective to be had, other than attacking for the sake of attacking. You can't even really expand your empire, because there are so few available troops. Holding land is pointless anyway, because it's just empty space. The game's only currency is faith, which is generated off board. The entirety of the experience is taking turns punching each other in the face, until the allotted number of punches are landed.

That sounds terrible. Yet my plays so far have been very enjoyable. There are a number of paths through the troop upgrades and the mythical monsters you can recruit keep combat interesting. Also, the card based combat system injects an almost perfect amount of player control, while still generating a fairly wide spread of results.

If you need a narrative arc, this game is not for you. If you want to admire the mighty empire you've built up from a tiny city-state at the end of the game, this one is not for you. This is a pure tactics game that rewards reactionary game play to a fault. If you can get around the lack of a storyline motivation for the fighting and look past the complete non-existance of an actual goal, you'll find a very enjoyable game.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8
In my younger and more innocent years, I used to play a fair bit of Risk. I like Risk...or rather, I like the idea behind Risk: march your armies across the board and crush your enemies beneath your heel.

As I got a little older and became more self-aware, I realized that while I enjoy this concept, Risk itself took too long to deliver and often boiled down to a few simple strategies. And it could easily leave some players sitting on their thumbs for an afternoon.

Kemet is a game that feels like the designer said: "You know what would make Risk fun? If it only took 2 hours, you could upgrade your army and you were encouraged attack, attack, attack!" That's what this game delivers.

Where Chaos in the Old World is a game where each player begins entrenched in their play style, Kemet starts everyone on even footing and then lets each player dictate their own play style as the game progresses. Wanna go for red cards, white cards or blue cards? What about a mix of all? Want better combat cards, or special troops?

It's a very clever spin on the dudes-on-a-map formula and is enhanced by some pretty delightful components. The "Mythological-Egypt" theme is pretty sweet, too. It may not quite be my favorite replacement for a Risk-like experience (just wait for it...) but, as it is, it's a great game.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-

RATING: N/A
Area control and confrontation with miniature armies in Ancient Egypt.  Spend prayer tokens to buy white, red, and blue upgrades or increase the level of each color's pyramid. Identical player combat decks are played to modify combat. A big decision for players is when to use or save their big combat cards.  What shines in this game is the anti-turtling measures employed by dividing territories in a way that allows for fast travel, by allowing forces to attack any region with obelisks, and with player pyramid action boards that limit action choices which nudges players into conflict.  A big part of the game is learning how the technology upgrade tiles work and how to use them most effectively.

I can't in good conscience give a brand new game, that i only played once, a perfect 10. But i am going to toe that line with a 9.5! This game is thrilling, agonizing, visually stunning, instinctive, carnal, crisp and consice, etc. I could go on. 
If you enjoy confrontational games, this is your game. If you enjoy agonizing over multiple great tactical/strategic choices, this is your game. If you enjoy games with high quality figures and beautiful art, this is definetly your game. The one draw back to this game is the rules explination time. There is alot going on and new players will be overwhelmed by the available option right off the bat. Lots of icons to digest.
Just as a base line, my top choice games are St. Petersburg, Power Grid, Agricola and LOTR: The card game.
So you can see that i love a good euro. This game fills that "wanna crush my opponent to a pulp" void that most euros just cant satisfy. BTW, one cannot be completely eliminated. You can simply resurrect any number of troops on your next turn for a cost in 'prayer' points. Brilliant! Oh and, once you control a creature, it is yours for the rest of the game, which will grant your unit special abilities. The creature figures completely stole the show. Fantastic production quality with some kind of glaze that excentuates the details of the figures. Jaw dropping! Also, the game uses three colors of 4 sided dice as pyramids which corrispond to three different class of upgrades. Each time you level up your blue pyrimid, you then have access to a new class of upgrades for that corrisponding color.
Anywayz, ranting. 'love the game. gonna buy ASAP (Feb?). 

P.S. Thanks Eric for introducing us to this game! Great job with the rule explination. Trust me, i know it as difficult to teach a new game as it is to learn one. And thanks to Pheonix Games in Mukilteo, WA. I just discovered this little game shop a few weeks ago. Great atmosphere, great selection, great people!

-Chris

RATING: 8.4
I have wanted to play this game for ages and now I finally have I'm not disappointed with it.
It's a simple case of do or die and I love how it's isn't convoluted and there's no messing: it's just down to action.
There's plenty of strategy but it's difficult to get anywhere if you are playing someone who has an idea what they are doing and you don't (it will feel lame) but once you know all the tiles you can buy and what they do you should pick up nicely.
LOADS of take that so if you don't like that then I don't see this game going down well.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 5
The colorful, stylish font on the cover is my favorite part of the game.  It doesn't even matter that it's unreadable.

Our session dragged at the start because everyone focused on tech-ing up their units, which admittedly is the right thing to do. If everyone jumps into combat without upgrades, battle becomes an uninteresting exercise between closely-matched military groups.

The monster sculpts and powers are fun, especially when they're on your side, but that's not a good reason to purchase or play the game. [i]Kemet[/i] also suffers from the two most common multiplayer deficiencies: leader-bashing and hammering of the weak (i.e., the recently attacked).  In this case, the deficiencies are compounded by free-for-all board teleportation.

I'd also wager that some upgrade tiles are significantly better than others.  Since all upgrades are available each game, the tiles that are purchased by players early in turn order may become scripted.

In short, [i]Kemet[/i] is another light multiplayer combat game that isn't better than [i]Nexus Ops[/i].

RATING: 7
Great production quality as one would expect from the publisher is a great draw to Kemet... but the advancement/building/combat game in the box would be a ton of fun with wooden cubes for dudes and a flimsy deck of cards for tiles... though print out the player aids if you hate passing one tiny book around the table constantly.

The advancements are fun and give you neat abilities... the action and resource starvation is just right to make it thinky without constantly making your plans impossible... and the aggression is so tied to gaining points that you can hardly get angry with folks for "picking on you" if you leave yourself in a vulnerable position.  Lots of fun here if you like Euro mechanics and area control tied to blind bidding combat.

RATING: 9
Kártyavédő: 42 db 57x88 47 db 45x68
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 9
A good game that looks gorgeous. Top quality as usual with this company. Actual gameplay is done very well. No dice or luckfest for this light wargame. In fact the only luck comes from a deck of cards that every player gets one card of each turn. But these cards themselves are balanced. Battle is done like in Agot and is rewarded/encouraged with vp. It’s an aggressive game that punishes turtling. It includes some good ideas, retreating lets you take back your soldiers to the pool for power instead of them being chased for easy vp by others.  Moving your armies around commanded by huge monsters is fun and imaginative. All technologies are available from the start and open the way for many different routes. The tech tiles seem to be balanced very well. 

Minor nitpick: they should have included more overviews of all techs for every player.

RATING: 7
